The Objection of Foolishness in Sending Prophets and Its Refutation
Baqillani presents and refutes the objection that sending messengers to disbelievers is foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that sending prophets to those who will reject them is foolish, using a dialectical argument that turns the opponent's reasoning against itself.

Core Idea
If sending prophets is foolish, then so is creating disbelievers and giving them intellect.

This lesson presents a classic objection against prophethood: that it would be foolish for God to send messengers to people He knows will reject them. The objection seems plausible, but Baqillani systematically dismantles it by showing that the same logic would force absurd conclusions about creation and divine wisdom. Pay close attention to how he turns the opponent's own reasoning against them, leading to a powerful defense of prophetic missions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection carefully. The opponent claims it is foolish (safah) for God to send messengers to those He knows will disbelieve and be punished.
The term 'safah' implies irrational or undignified behavior.
2
Note Baqillani's initial distinction
Baqillani first points out that the objection does not apply to those who will accept the messengers. So the premise is not universal.
3
Follow the turning argument
Baqillani then argues that if God cannot send messengers to disbelievers, then by the same logic He cannot create them or give them intellects, since He knows they will reject those too.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the opponent's counter and Baqillani's final reply
The opponent tries to salvage their position by distinguishing between creation and prophetic obligation. Baqillani shows the distinction fails because both are for the sake of exposing the person to benefit.
The final rebuttal closes the dialectic: the opponent's reasoning would also undermine intellectual arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول آخر فإن قالوا : الدليل على أنه لا يجوز في حكمة الله سبحانه إرسال الرسل أن إرساله الرسل إلى من يعلم أنه يكفر به ويشتمه ويرد قوله ويستوجب بذلك العقاب الأليم سفه وخلاف الصواب فلما لم يجز السفه على الله سبحانه لم يجز أن يرسل الرسل إلى من حاله ما وصفنا فيقال لهم : أول ما في هذا أنه يجب جواز إرسال الله تعالى الرسل إلى من يعلم قبوله منهم وانتفاعه بهم لأن هذه العلة عنهم زائلة ثم يقال لهم : فيجب على اعتلالكم ألا يخلق الله سبحانه من يعلم أنه يكفر به ويجحد نعمه ويلحد في صفاته ولا ينتفع بوجود نفسه وألا يحتج بالعقول وما وضعه من الأدلة فيها على أحد علم أنه يجحدها ولا يستعملها ولا ينيب إلى ما وضح في عقله حسنه ولا يحذر مما حذر منه فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه نقضوا اعتلالهم . وإن قالوا إنما خلق من يعلم أنه يكفر واحتج عليه بعقله مع العلم بأنه لا يقبل ما كلفه بعقله تعريضاً منه للقبول وحسن الانتفاع به إذا كان منهم . قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكلّف على ألسنة الرسل من علم أنه يكفر ولا ينتفع إذا قصد بذلك تعريضه لنفع لا يصل إليه إلا بالتكليف السمعي وإن علم أنه يخالف ولا يقبل فإن قالوا : علمه بأنه لا يقبل يمنع من حسن النظر له بإنفاذ الرسل إليه ، قيل لهم : وكذلك علمه بأنه لا يقبل حجج العقول ولا ينظر ولا يختار إلا الإلحاد وفعل الظلم والعدوان يمنع من حسن النظر له بإقامة حجة العقل عليه وتكليف المصير إليها ولا جواب لهم ذلك
Another saying: For if they say: The proof that it is not permissible in the wisdom of God Almighty to send messengers is that sending messengers to one whom He knows will disbelieve in Him, revile Him, reject His word, and thereby deserve painful punishment is foolishness and contrary to correctness. So when foolishness is not permissible for God Almighty, it is not permissible for Him to send messengers to one whose state is as we described. It is said to them: The first point in this is that it is necessary to permit God Almighty to send messengers to those whom He knows will accept them and benefit from them, because this reason is absent from them. Then it is said to them: According to your reasoning, it would be necessary that God Almighty not create one whom He knows will disbelieve in Him, deny His blessings, blaspheme His attributes, and not benefit from his own existence, and that He not present proofs through the intellect and the evidences He placed therein to anyone He knows will deny them, not use them, not turn to what is clear in his intellect as good, and not fear what He warned against. For if they persist in that, they abandon their religion, and if they reject it, they refute their own reasoning. And if they say: He only creates one whom He knows will disbelieve and argues against him with his intellect, knowing that he will not accept what He imposes on him through his intellect, as an exposure to acceptance and the good of benefiting from him if he is among them. It is said to them: Then why do you not also deny that He imposes obligations through the tongues of messengers upon one whom He knows will disbelieve and not benefit, if by that He intends to expose him to a benefit that he cannot attain except through revealed obligation, even if He knows he will oppose and not accept? For if they say: His knowledge that he will not accept prevents the goodness of considering him by sending messengers to him. It is said to them: Likewise, His knowledge that he will not accept the arguments of the intellect, nor consider, nor choose anything but atheism and the commission of oppression and aggression, prevents the goodness of considering him by presenting the proofs of the intellect to him and obligating him to adhere to them. And they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer dialectic concerning the permissibility of sending prophets to those who will reject them. Baqillani first presents the objection: it would be foolish (safah) for God to send messengers to people He knows will disbelieve and incur punishment, so it cannot be from God. The response begins by noting that the objection only applies to those who will reject, but there are also those who will accept. Then Baqillani turns the opponent's reasoning against them: if God cannot send messengers to disbelievers, then by the same logic He cannot create disbelievers at all, nor can He give them intellects and arguments, since He knows they will reject them. The opponent then tries to salvage their position by saying that creation and intellectual argument are for the sake of exposure to acceptance. Baqillani responds by asking why the same argument cannot apply to prophetic obligation: it is also an exposure to benefit. The opponent retorts that knowledge of non-acceptance prevents the goodness of sending messengers. Baqillani closes by pointing out that this same reasoning would also prevent the goodness of intellectual arguments, since God also knows they will be rejected. Thus the opponent's position is self-defeating. Baqillani concludes that they have no answer.

Key Terms
سفه
رسل
عقل
تكليف
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's foreknowledge of disbelief nullifies the wisdom of sending prophets, since they will reject anyway.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the same logic would nullify all divine actions, including creation and giving intellect. The real purpose is to expose the person to benefit, not to guarantee acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's response, what would the opponent's reasoning necessarily entail regarding creation?
A) God should create only believers
B) God should not create those He knows will disbelieve
C) God should not create anyone
D) God should only create angels

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection that sending prophets to disbelievers is foolish by showing that the same logic would invalidate creation and intellect, and that divine actions are for exposing benefit, not guaranteeing acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter arguments that seem to question the wisdom of God's actions, do you immediately trust His wisdom, or do you find yourself needing to intellectually resolve the tension? What does this reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
This passage shows that God sends messengers even to those who will reject, for exposure to benefit. How does this challenge your own tendency to avoid sharing truth with people you assume will not accept it? Are you willing to be a means of exposure, regardless of the outcome?
?
Baqillani ties together creation, intellect, and prophecy as consistent acts of divine wisdom. Are there areas in your life where you compartmentalize God's work—seeing His hand in nature but not in revelation, or vice versa? How can you begin to see all of life as flowing from the same wise God?

The Wisdom of Islamic Rituals: Baqillani's Response
Baqillani presents and begins to refute the objection that rituals are irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends the wisdom of Islamic rituals against the charge of irrationality by shifting the basis of wisdom from human recognition to divine knowledge and using analogy with worldly pursuits.

Core Idea
Rituals are wise because God knows they lead to greater good, even if their benefit is not immediately apparent.

This passage presents a strong objection against prophethood: how can a wise God command seemingly useless rituals like Sa'i, Tawaf, and fasting? Baqillani responds by shifting the basis of wisdom from human recognition to divine knowledge. He argues that if God knows these acts lead to benefit—such as calling people to tawhid and reward—then they are wise, just like difficult worldly journeys undertaken for profit. Although the response is cut short, the core argument is clear.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. The objectors claim that specific Islamic rituals are ugly (qabih) because they involve hardship and there is no rational difference between sacred and ordinary places. List the rituals mentioned.
The objection targets Sa'i, Tawaf, kissing the Black Stone, fasting, and abstaining from pleasures.
2
Identify Baqillani's counter
Notice how Baqillani begins his response: 'ما أنكرتم أن يكون ذلك أجمع حكمة' – 'What prevents you from considering all of this as wisdom?' He questions the assumption that wisdom must be apparent to human reason.
This is a key turning point.
3
Analyze the analogy
Baqillani compares rituals to difficult worldly endeavors like crossing the sea or desert for profit. Why is this analogy effective? Think about how hardship can be considered good if it leads to a greater benefit.
The analogy shows that benefit justifies hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول آخر لهم فإن قالوا : الدليل على فساد الرسالة قبح السعي بين الصفا والمروة والطواف بالبيت وتقبيل الحجر والجوع والعطش في أيام الصيام والمنع من فعل الملاذ التي تُصلح الأجسام وأنه لا فرق بين البيت الحرام وبين غيره وبين الصفا والمروة وبين غيرها من البقاع وبين عرفة وبين غيرها فثبت أن ذلك أجمع ليس من أوامر الحكيم سبحانه ، يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون ذلك أجمع حكمةٌ إذا علم الله سبحانه أن فعله والتعبد به صلاح لكثير من خلقه وداع لهم إلى فعل توحيده والثناء عليه بصفاته وما هو أهله وغير ذلك مما ينالون به جزيل ثوابه وأن يكون ذلك بمنزلة حسن ركوب البحر وقطع المهمة القفر في طلب الرفد والربح وبمنزلة عدو الإنسان بجهده
Another saying of them: For if they say: The proof of the corruption of prophethood is the ugliness of Sa'i between Safa and Marwa, circumambulation of the House, kissing the Black Stone, hunger and thirst on days of fasting, and prevention from indulging in pleasures that benefit the body, and that there is no difference between the Sacred House and other places, between Safa and Marwa and other places, between Arafah and other places. So it is established that all of this is not from the commands of the Wise, Exalted is He. It is said to them: What prevents you from considering that all of this is wisdom if God Almighty knows that performing it and worshipping through it is beneficial for many of His creation and a call for them to perform His tawhid, praise Him with His attributes and what He deserves, and other things by which they attain His abundant reward, and that this is like the goodness of riding the sea and crossing the empty desert in search of gifts and profit, and like a person's enemy with his effort...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection against prophethood, this time focusing on specific Islamic rituals: Sa'i, Tawaf, kissing the Black Stone, fasting, and abstaining from pleasures. The objectors claim these actions are inherently ugly (qabih) and serve no rational purpose, and since there is no difference between these places and ordinary locations, they cannot be from God. Baqillani begins his response by turning the tables: how do you know that these acts are not wisdom? If God knows that these acts lead to benefit for many people—including calling them to tawhid, praise, and reward—then they are like other difficult actions that people undertake for worldly gain, such as crossing the sea or desert for profit. The response is cut off, but the initial argument is that the wisdom of an action depends on divine knowledge of its outcomes, not on inherent rational recognizability. Baqillani also uses analogy: just as people endure hardship for worldly benefit, the same can be true for religious benefit.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rituals must have an inherent rational purpose to be from God.
Correction: Baqillani argues that their wisdom depends on God's knowledge of their beneficial outcomes, not on human recognition of intrinsic rationality.

A) The beauty of the Kaaba
B) Crossing the sea and desert for profit
C) The taste of honey

Key Takeaway
Rituals are wise because God knows they lead to greater good, even if their wisdom is not apparent to human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there Islamic practices you perform (e.g., fasting, prayer, Hajj rituals) that sometimes feel 'irrational' or without obvious benefit? How does Baqillani's argument challenge you to trust God's wisdom over your own perception? What would change in your worship if you deeply internalized that God sees a benefit you cannot see?
?

Can you, right now, identify at least one potential spiritual fruit (like drawing closer to tawhid, or attaining reward) that might be hidden in that act? Will you commit to performing it this week with that intention, asking Allah to show you His wisdom?
?
The objectors argued that there is no difference between the Sacred House and any other place. When you enter the mosque or face the Kaaba in prayer, do you truly feel a sense of distinction? What concrete step can you take to cultivate reverence for the sacred symbols of Islam, even if the world around you sees them as ordinary?

Refutation of the Objection: Does God's Foreknowledge of Disbelief Invalidate Sending Prophets?
Baqillani presents and refutes an objection about God's foreknowledge of disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection that God's foreknowledge of a person's rejection makes sending prophets futile or unjust.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge of rejection does not invalidate the goodness of sending prophets.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses a key objection raised against the necessity of prophethood: if God already knows that a person will disbelieve and reject the message, why send prophets? The objectors argue that such foreknowledge would render the sending pointless and even unjust. Baqillani turns the argument around by applying the same logic to rational proofs, showing the objection is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objection is presented first, then the answer.
2
Identify the objector's claim
What is the core of the objection? They claim that God's knowledge of rejection prevents the goodness of sending prophets.
This is a common theological pitfall.
3
See how Baqillani refutes it
He uses a tuq argument: the same logic applies to rational proofs. If foreknowledge of rejection invalidates sending prophets, it also invalidates rational evidence. Since the objectors accept rational proofs, they are inconsistent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that God can send prophets even to those He knows will reject, because the benefit lies in the offer itself, not necessarily in acceptance.
This preserves both divine justice and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكلّف على ألسنة الرسل من علم أنه يكفر ولا ينتفع إذا قصد بذلك تعريضه لنفع لا يصل إليه إلا بالتكليف السمعي وإن علم أنه يخالف ولا يقبل؟ فإن قالوا : عِلْمُه بأنه لا يقبل يمنع من حسن النظر له بإنفاذ الرسل إليه ، قيل لهم : وكذلك علمه بأنه لا يقبل حجج العقول ولا ينظر ولا يختار إلا الإلحاد وفعل الظلم والعدوان يمنع من حسن النظر له بإقامة حجة العقل عليه وتكليف المصير إليها ؛ ولا جواب لهم ذلك.
They were told: 'What prevents you also from [acknowledging] that He may charge on the tongues of the messengers one whom He knows will disbelieve and not benefit, if by that He intends to expose him to a benefit that he cannot attain except through the revealed charge, even though He knows that he will oppose and not accept?' If they say: 'His knowledge that he will not accept prevents the goodness of considering for him by sending messengers to him,' they are told: 'Similarly, His knowledge that he will not accept the proofs of intellects, and will not consider, and will choose only atheism and oppression and transgression, prevents the goodness of considering for him by establishing the proof of the intellect upon him and charging him to adopt it. And they have no answer to that.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objectors argue that if God knows a person will not accept the prophetic message and will disbelieve, then sending messengers to such a person would be futile and therefore ugly (qabīḥ). The response uses a tuq (turning the tables) argument: the same logic would apply to rational proofs—if God knows a person will reject intellectual arguments and persist in disbelief and injustice, then creating intellect and providing rational evidence would also be futile and ugly. Since the objectors presumably affirm that rational proofs are valid and good, they must also affirm the goodness of sending messengers despite foreknowledge of rejection. The author concludes that the objectors have no valid answer to this.

Key Terms
تكليف
علم
حسن النظر
حجة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's foreknowledge of a person's rejection makes sending a messenger to that person pointless or unjust.
Correction: The same logic would apply to rational proofs, which the objectors accept, so the objection is self-refuting. The benefit of sending prophets is not contingent on acceptance but on the offer of guidance and the test itself.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's response, what is the consequence of the objectors' logic regarding rational proofs?
A) Rational proofs would also be invalid if foreknowledge of rejection prevents goodness.
B) Rational proofs remain valid despite foreknowledge.
C) Rational proofs depend on acceptance.
D) Rational proofs are irrelevant to prophethood.

Key Takeaway
God's foreknowledge of rejection does not nullify the goodness of sending prophets; the same logic refutes the objection by showing it would equally undermine rational evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors claimed that God's foreknowledge of rejection makes sending prophets futile. Yet the author refutes this by pointing out that the same logic would undermine rational proofs, which they accept. In your own life, do you find yourself more willing to trust your intellect over revelation when both point to the same truth? What would it take for you to submit fully to prophetic guidance even when your reason hesitates? Commit to examining one teaching of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that you have intellectually doubted and seek clarity with an open heart.
?
This lesson teaches that the act of conveying guidance is good regardless of whether it is accepted. Think of someone you know who has rejected truth despite your efforts. Have you ever felt discouraged or thought your efforts were wasted? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? Will you recommit to sharing the truth with them, not expecting results but trusting that the act itself is valuable in God's sight?
?
The parallel drawn between prophetic and rational evidence suggests that both are from God and both are valid means of guidance. Are you treating the Quran and the intellect as complementary or contradictory? Identify one area where you rely more on your own reasoning than on revelation. How can you restore the proper balance? Make a concrete plan to increase your engagement with revelation in that area.

Refuting the Charge of Irrational Rituals
Baqillani refutes the objection that Hajj and fasting rituals are irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection that specific religious rituals (like sa'y, tawaf, fasting) are irrational and therefore cannot be from a wise God.

Core Idea
Rituals are wise because they bring spiritual benefit, just as worldly hardships are undertaken for gain.

This passage presents a second objection from those who deny prophethood by claiming that the specific rites of Hajj and fasting are inherently ugly (qabīḥ) and indiscernible from other actions. Al-Baqillani responds by showing that these acts are filled with divine wisdom: they lead to tawhid, reward, and spiritual betterment, analogous to accepted worldly practices like traveling for profit or fleeing danger. He also argues that God's wisdom is not constrained by human notions of inherent distinction, and that the benefit to the believer justifies the command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objectors state their case. Notice the key claim: the rituals are 'ugly' because they involve hardship and lack inherent distinction.
The objection focuses on qabīḥ (ugliness) as a rational judgment.
2
See the first response: benefitting creatures
Baqillani counters that these rituals are wise because God knows they benefit His creatures—they lead to tawhid, praise, and reward. He compares them to worldly hardships undertaken for gain.
This is a teleological argument: ends justify means.
3
Examine the second response: God's prerogative
Even if the acts are not inherently distinct, God could command them and it wouldn't contradict wisdom. Baqillani uses the analogy of a wise builder who destroys and rebuilds for benefit.
This addresses the 'no difference' argument.
4
Consider the deeper analogy: spiritual betterment vs. destruction
Baqillani argues that if these rituals bring spiritual betterment (salāḥ), then charging with them is good—even better than destroying the believer's life and senses.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the objector's logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول آخر لهم: فإن قالوا : الدليل على فساد الرسالة قبح السعي بين الصفا والمروة والطواف بالبيت وتقبيل الحجر والجوع والعطش في أيام الصيام والمنع من فعل الملاذُ التي تُصلح الأجسام وأنه لا فرق بين البيت الحرام وبين غيره وبين الصفا والمَرْوَةِ وبين غيرها من البقاع وبين عَرَفَةَ وبين غيرها فثبت أن ذلك أجمع ليس من أوامر الحكيم سبحانه، يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون ذلك أجمع حكمةٌ إذا علم الله سبحانه أن فِعله والتعبد به صلاح لكثير من خلقه وداع لهم إلى فعل توحيده والثناء عليه بصفاته وما هو أهله وغير ذلك مما ينالون به جزيل ثوابه وأن يكون ذلك بمنزلة حُسن ركوب البحر وقطع المهمّة القفر في طلب الرفدِ والربح وبمنزلة عدو الإنسان بجهده وطاقته في الحَزْن والوَعْرِ من الأرض خوفاً من السبع وممن يريد قتله وسَفْكَ دمه ظلماً وأخذ ماله وقبح ذلك منه إذا لم يفعله لاجتلاب منفعة ولا دفع مَضَرَّةٍ. وأما قولكم إنه لا فرق بين الصفا والمَرْوَة والسعي بينهما وبين غيرهما ولا بين البيت الحرام وبين غيره فهو كما وصفتم ولو شاء الله أن يتعبد بالسعي في كل بقعة والتوجه إلى كل جهة لساغ ذلك منه إذا عرض به لثوابه ولم يكن ذلك ناقضاً لحكمته. ويقال لهم : وكذلك لم تجدوا حكيماً بنى أحسن البنيان وصوّر أكمل الصور وأشرفها ثم نقضها وهدم صورها وقبحها وذهب ببهجتها وشوه خلقها ؛ فإن قالوا : إذا كان في ذلك مصلحة المخلوق جاز تغيير خلقه وقَلْبُ صفته ومحـو محاسنه ، قيل لهم : وكذلك إذا كان صوم النهار وقيام الليل وتقبيل الحجر والطواف والسعي ورمي الجمار يعود بصلاح المكلّفِ حسن تكليفه وكان ذلك أحسن في العقل إن كان فيه حسن من إتلاف نفس المكلف وإبطال حياته وهدم صورته ومحو محاسنه وإبطال عقله وحواسه ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك.
Another statement of theirs: For they said: 'The proof of the corruption of prophethood is the ugliness of the sa'y between Safa and Marwa, and the tawaf around the House, and kissing the Stone, and hunger and thirst on the days of fasting, and the prohibition of pleasures that benefit the bodies, and that there is no difference between the Sacred House and other [houses], and between Safa and Marwa and other places, and between Arafat and other [places], so it is established that all of that is not among the commands of the Wise (subhanahu).' It is said to them: 'What prevents you from [acknowledging] that all of that is wisdom, if God (subhanahu) knows that doing it and worshipping through it is beneficial for many of His creatures, and an invitation for them to perform His tawhid, and praise Him with His attributes and what He deserves, and other things by which they attain His abundant reward, and that that is like the goodness of riding the sea and traversing the barren desert in seeking sustenance and profit, and like the running of a man with his effort and strength on rough and difficult ground, out of fear of a wild beast or of one who wants to kill him and shed his blood unjustly and take his property, and that [running] would be ugly of him if he did not do it for acquiring a benefit or repelling a harm.' As for your statement that there is no difference between Safa and Marwa and sa'y between them and other [places], and between the Sacred House and other [houses], it is as you described, and if God had willed to command worship through sa'y in every place and facing every direction, that would be permissible from Him if He exposed [them] thereby to His reward, and that would not contradict His wisdom. And it is said to them: 'Likewise, you do not find a wise person who builds the best buildings and creates the most perfect and noble forms, then destroys them and ruins their forms and makes them ugly and removes their beauty and disfigures their creation.' If they say: 'If in that there is a benefit for the creature, it is permissible to change his creation, transform his attribute, and efface his beauties,' they are told: 'Likewise, if the fasting of the day and night prayer and kissing the Stone and tawaf and sa'y and throwing the pebbles result in the betterment of the charged person, then his being charged is good, and that is more good in reason, if there is good in it, than destroying the soul of the charged person, nullifying his life, ruining his form, effacing his beauties, and destroying his intellect and senses. And they have no answer to that.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objectors claim that the specific rituals of Hajj and fasting are ugly (qabīḥ) because they involve hardship and seem arbitrary with no inherent distinction from other acts; therefore, they cannot be commands of a wise God. The refutation proceeds in several steps: (1) These rituals are wise because God knows they benefit His creatures—they lead to tawhid, praise, and reward—just as worldly hardships like sea travel or fleeing from danger are undertaken for benefit, and would be ugly if done for no reason. (2) Even if the acts are not inherently different from others, God could command them if He wills, and that would not contradict wisdom. (3) An analogy is drawn to a wise person who builds and then destroys a structure if there is benefit; similarly, if these rituals bring about spiritual betterment, then charging the believer with them is good—even better than destroying the believer's life and senses. The passage ends by stating the objectors have no answer.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religious rituals are inherently meaningless or arbitrary.
Correction: The rituals have divine wisdom: they serve as means for spiritual benefit, analogous to accepted worldly practices.

Checkpoint
What analogies does Baqillani use to defend the rituals of Hajj and fasting?

C) Both A and B.
D) None; he only uses logical argument.

Key Takeaway
The rituals of Hajj and fasting are not arbitrary but are divinely commanded means for spiritual benefit, analogous to accepted worldly hardships.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever performed a ritual like tawaf or fasting while feeling it was pointless? When you feel the hardship, do you trust that God has a purpose for you in it? If not, what is holding you back from that trust?
?
You endure discomfort for worldly gains like career or health. Why do you often resist similar discomfort in worship? Is it because you doubt the spiritual return? Answer honestly: do you truly believe the reward of the afterlife is greater than any worldly profit?
?
The objectors said: 'there is no difference between the Sacred House and other houses.' Similarly, you may feel no connection to certain acts of worship. But God chose them for you. Will you accept His choice out of love and obedience, or will you insist on your own rational judgment? What would change in your prayer if you approached it as a divinely chosen gift rather than a routine?

Proof of the Possibility and Wisdom of Sending Prophets
Baqillani argues that sending prophets is permissible and wise, given the benefits and lack of harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that sending prophets is both permissible and wise, based on the absence of harm and the presence of great benefit.

Core Idea
If prophethood corrupts nothing and benefits all, it is a wise act of God.

Baqillani presents a concise proof for the validity of prophethood. He lists six things that must be absent for sending prophets to be unproblematic, and then highlights the immense reward that results. This argument shows that prophethood is not only possible but also a perfect expression of divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the six conditions that must be absent
The passage lists six things that sending prophets cannot entail: corruption of obligation, nullification of trial, reversal of proofs, bringing the Eternal out of eternity, alteration of realities, and attributing deficiency to God. Find each one in the Arabic.
These conditions protect God's attributes and the consistency of His actions.
3
Recognize the positive benefit
After listing the negatives, Baqillani states that sending prophets exposes the obligated to immense reward and great benefit. This is the positive purpose.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since sending prophets is free from all those harms and brings great good, it is correct in divine wisdom and for the sake of creation. This shows it is permissible and wise, not necessary.
The phrase 'wa kana li-khalq Allah' means it is for the creation, not for God's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على صحة إرسال الله تعالى الرسل وجوازه هو أنه إذا لم يكن في إرسالهم إفساد التكليف ولا إبطال المحنة ولا إيجاب قلب بعض الأدلة ولا إخراج القديم عن قدمه ولا قلب لبعض الحقائق ولا إلحاق صفة النقص بالمرسل جل ذكره، وكان في إرساله تعريض ذلك المكلفين لثواب جزيل ونفع عظيم، صح في حكمته وكان لخلق الله.
And among what indicates the correctness of sending messengers by God Most High and its permissibility is that if in sending them there is no corruption of obligation, nor nullification of trial, nor necessitation of reversing some proofs, nor bringing the Eternal out of His eternity, nor alteration of some realities, nor attaching the attribute of deficiency to the Sender (mighty is His mention), and in His sending there is exposure of those obligated to immense reward and great benefit, then it is correct in His wisdom and it is for the creation of God.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof for the possibility and necessity of prophethood. Baqillani argues that sending prophets is valid if it does not entail any of the following: (1) corrupting the obligation (تكليف), (2) nullifying the divine trial (محنة), (3) requiring the reversal of some evidences, (4) bringing the Eternal out of His eternity, (5) altering any realities, or (6) attributing deficiency to God. Since sending prophets does none of these, and instead brings immense reward and benefit to those who are obligated, it is consistent with divine wisdom. The phrase 'وكان لخلق الله' indicates that it is for the sake of creation. This argument establishes that prophethood is not only possible but also a wise act of God.

Key Terms

تكليف
محنة
قديم
حقيقة
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof claims prophethood is necessary for God; rather it shows it is permissible and wise, given His attributes.
Correction: Baqillani argues that sending prophets is permissible (jā'iz) and correct in wisdom (ṣaḥḥa fī ḥikmatih), not obligatory upon God.

Checkpoint

A) That it brings reward to the believers.
B) That it corrupts the obligation or nullifies the trial.
C) That it is difficult for the messengers.
D) That the prophets are infallible.

Key Takeaway
Sending prophets is permissible and wise because it brings great benefit without causing any harm to God's attributes or creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof concludes that sending prophets is a wise act that exposes you to great reward. Are you living as if you truly believe that? What would change today if you fully embraced the accountability and reward that prophethood brings?

The Necessity of Revelation for Knowledge of Poisons
Baqillani refutes the claim that empirical knowledge of poisons is reliable without divine guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that knowledge of poisons and medicine requires divine revelation, not mere experience.

Core Idea
Experience alone cannot guarantee certainty about delayed effects; only revelation provides sure knowledge.

Baqillani challenges the assumption that empirical testing of poisons yields reliable knowledge. He points out the variability in the timing of effects and asks a pointed question that undermines the claim. This passage sets the stage for his broader argument on the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a distinction of timing, a question, a conclusion.
The passage begins in the middle of a sentence; the footnoted variants show missing words, but the main argument is intact.
2
Follow the argument about variable timing
Baqillani lists different times when a poison can kill: instantly, after a day, after a month, after a year. Then he asks: how can you be sure that a death after a delay is due to the poison, and not something else?
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the opponent cannot provide assurance, Baqillani concludes that reason alone cannot provide certainty. Therefore, revelation (sam' and tawqif) is necessary. He also notes that this view is held by the majority of Muslims and many from other religions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوم يقتل لوقته وساعته ومنه ليومه ومنه ما يقتل بعد شهر وحول؟ فإن قالوا : نعم، قيل لهم : فما الذي يُؤمنُنا من أن يكون تَلَف ذلك الحيوان بعد أو شهر أو سنة من تأثير تلك الثمرة وعمل تلك الحشيشة وإننا لا نأمن أن يكون ما جربناه عليه قاتلاً بعد سنة فما الأمان لنا عند أكله الاستضرار والتلف بعده بوقت أو أوقات؟ فلا يجدون سبيلا إلى الخلاص من ذلك . وهذا يدلُّ على بطلان ما تعلَّقوا به فوجب أن يكون العلم بهذا الشأن الجسيم والخطب العظيم غير مُنال ولا مُدْرَكِ من جهة العقل وأن الناس محتاجون في علم ذلك إلى سمع وتوقيف وأن الواجب على أصولهم أن يكون العلم بأصل الطب موقفا عليه ومأخوذاً من جهة الرسل عليهم السلام وإن قيس على ذلك واحْتُذِي عليه وعلى هذا أكثر الأمة وكثير ممن خالفهم من أهل الملل.
A [poison] kills at its moment and hour, and some kills on its day, and some kills after a month and a year? If they say: Yes, it is said to them: What then secures us from the destruction of that animal after a day, a month, or a year from the effect of that fruit and the action of that herb? And we are not secure that what we have experienced as killing after a year—so what security do we have when eating it from harm and destruction after a time or times? So they find no way to escape from that. And this indicates the invalidity of what they clung to, so it must be that knowledge of this grave matter and great calamity is not attainable nor perceivable from the intellect, and that people are in need for knowledge of that to [revealed] hearing and authoritative instruction, and that it is obligatory according to their principles that knowledge of the foundation of medicine be dependent upon and taken from the messengers (peace be upon them), and if measured upon that and modeled upon it, and upon this [view] is the majority of the [Muslim] community and many of those who oppose them from the people of [other] religions.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that empirical knowledge—specifically the knowledge of poisons and their effects—can be reliably obtained through reason and experience alone, without prophetic revelation. Baqillani begins by distinguishing degrees of lethal effect: some poisons kill instantly, some within a day, some after a month, and some after a year. He then poses a question to his opponents who accept the variability of timing: what assurance do you have that a particular animal's death after a delay—perhaps a day, month, or year—is not due to the effect of a fruit or herb you have tested? How can you be certain that what you have experienced as fatal only after a year might not kill sooner or later on another occasion? Since the opponent cannot provide such assurance, they have no way to argue for the reliability of unaided empirical inference. Baqillani then draws the conclusion: this demonstrates the falsehood of their position, and proves that knowledge of such grave matters (like medicine) cannot be attained through reason alone; rather, people need 'hearing' (sam') and authoritative instruction (tawqif), meaning revelation from prophets. He further notes that, on the opponents' own principles, even the foundational knowledge of medicine would have to be derived from prophets, and this view is held by the majority of the Muslim community and many from other religious traditions.

Key Terms
سمع
توقيف
عقل
جرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that empirical science is fully reliable without revelation.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the variability of effects (delayed impact) undermines certainty, so revelation is needed for sure knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why cannot empirical knowledge of poisons provide certainty?
A) Because poisons never kill instantly, only after long delays.
B) Because the timing of death from a poison can vary, so one cannot be sure when or if it will kill.
C) Because reason is completely useless and cannot be trusted at all.
D) Because prophets disagreed on the effects of poisons.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of poisons and medicine requires divine guidance because experience cannot account for variable and delayed effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you rely more on your own experience or scientific consensus than on divine guidance—and does Baqillani's argument make you question that reliance? What would it take for you to seek a clear prophetic instruction in that area?
?
When you face a decision where the possible harm is delayed or uncertain (e.g., dietary choices, medical treatments), do you feel the same level of security as when you follow a clear command from Allah? If not, what is the difference, and why do you accept that difference?
?
If you truly believed that certain knowledge about life-threatening matters can only come from revelation, how would that change your daily habits—from what you eat to the medicines you take? Are you willing to make that change starting today?

The Rational Possibility of Prophethood
Baqillani argues for the rational possibility and wisdom of sending prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why sending prophets is both possible and wise given God's attributes and the benefit to humanity.

Core Idea
Sending prophets is rational because it brings great good without contradicting God's perfection.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his proof of prophethood by establishing that sending prophets is not only possible but also entailed by divine wisdom. He argues that prophethood does not corrupt the system of obligation (taklif), annul trial, or contradict God's attributes. Instead, it offers immense rewards, making it wise for God to send messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if not... and there is... then it is valid.'
2
Identify the conditions that must be absent
Baqillani lists six things that would make prophethood problematic: corruption of taklif, annulment of trial, overturning proofs, removing God from eternality, overturning realities, and attaching deficiency to God. Notice how each is negated.
These conditions are all negative; their absence makes prophethood wise.
3
Understand the positive benefit
After listing the negated harms, Baqillani mentions the positive benefit: exposure to abundant reward. This combination (no harm + great benefit) justifies prophethood in divine wisdom.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that sending prophets is valid in God's wisdom and is from His act. This establishes that prophethood is rationally possible and wise, not arbitrary.
This lays the foundation for the second proof from tawatur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على صحة إرسال الله تعالى الرسل وجوازه هو أنه إذا لم يكن في إرسالهم إفساد التكليف ولا إبطال المحنة ولا إيجاب قلب بعض الأدلة ولا إخراج القديم عن قدمه ولا قلب لبعض الحقائق ولا إلحاق صفة النقص بالمُرْسِل جلّ ذكره وكان في إرساله تعريض ذلك المكلفين لثواب جزيل ونفع عظيم صح في حكمته وكان من فعله سبحانه ومما يدل على جواز إرسال الله الرسل وأنه قد فعل ذلك عِلْمُنا بأن اليهود والنصارى والمسلمين قد أطبقوا على نقل أعلام موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلّى الله عليه وعليهم وأن الكذب مستحيل جوازه على مثلهم من ناحية التراسل والتكاتب والمواطأة على ذلك لأن تمام ذلك وانتظامه من مثلهم محال مُتَعَذِّرٌ في العادة ومحال أيضاً جوازه على مثلهم بأن يجتمعوا جميعاً في بُقْعَةٍ واحدة من حيث يُشاهِدُ بعضُهم بعضاً ويتوافقوا على الكذب ونقله وإذاعته لأن اجتماع مثلهم في بقعة واحدة متعذر في مُسْتَقَرُّ العادة ولو أمكن أيضاً اجتماعهم لتَعَدّرَ في مُستقَرِّ العادة تَوَاطُوهم على الكذب ونقله واسْتِتارُ ذلك منهم وانْكِتامُه عليهم لأن العادة موضوعة على خلاف ذلك ويستحيل أيضاً وقوع الكذب من جماعةِ مَنْ ذكرنا من أعلام الرسل باتفاق وقوعه ؛ لأن العادة لم تجر باتفاق وقوع الكذب في مائة ألف إنسان عن مُخْبَرٍ واحد لداع واحد ودواع متفرقة وإن جاز ذلك الواحد والاثنين والنفر اليسير وليس يمكن وقوع الكذب من هذه الجماعات إلا على هذه الوجوه فإذا امتنعت فَسَدَ جواز الكذب عليهم ؛ وفي فساد ذلك إيجاب صدقهم فيما نقلوه وصحة ما إليه ذهبنا ولو أمكن وقوع الكذب مِنْ جميع من ذكرناه نَقَلَةِ أعلام الرسل على بعض هذه الوجوه أو غيرها لداع واحد أو دواع متباينة، لأمكن وقوعه من نَقَلَةِ الأمصار والبلدان والممالك من
And among what indicates the validity and possibility of God Most High sending messengers is that if there is not in their sending a corruption of obligation (taklif) nor an annulment of trial (mihnah) nor a necessity of overturning some proofs nor a removal of the Eternal from His eternality nor an overturning of some realities nor an attachment of the attribute of deficiency to the Sender—glorious is His mention—and in His sending there is an exposure of those obligated to abundant reward and great benefit, then it is valid in His wisdom and is from His act—glorified is He. And among what indicates the possibility of God sending messengers and that He has done that is our knowledge that the Jews, Christians, and Muslims have unanimously agreed upon the transmission of the miracles (a'lam) of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad—may God bless him and them—and that falsehood is impossible to occur from the likes of them with respect to correspondence, writing, and collusion upon that, because the completion and organization of that from the likes of them is impossible and unattainable in custom (adah), and it is also impossible for its occurrence from the likes of them that they all gather in one place where some see others and agree upon falsehood, its transmission, and its dissemination, because gathering of the likes of them in one place is impossible in the stable course of custom; and even if their gathering were also possible, their collusion upon falsehood and its transmission and the concealment of that from them and its suppression upon them would be impossible in the stable course of custom, because custom is established contrary to that. It is also impossible for falsehood to occur from the group of those we have mentioned from among the miracles of the messengers by agreement of its occurrence, because custom has not run upon the agreement of falsehood occurring from a hundred thousand people concerning a single informant (mukhbar) due to a single motive and separate motives, even if that is permissible for one, two, and a small number. And it is not possible for falsehood to occur from these groups except in these ways. So if they are impossible, the possibility of falsehood from them is invalid. And in the invalidity of that is the necessity of their truthfulness in what they transmitted and the correctness of what we have held. And if the occurrence of falsehood from all those we have mentioned—the transmitters of the miracles of the messengers—were possible in some of these ways or others due to a single motive or differing motives, then its occurrence from the transmitters of the cities, countries, and kingdoms would be possible.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility and actuality of prophethood, and the truthfulness of the prophets. Baqillani presents two main arguments. First, he gives a rational argument: sending messengers does not corrupt the system of obligation (taklif) or trial (mihnah), nor does it conflict with God's attributes or truth; rather, it brings great reward, so it is wise and possible for God to do so. Second, he gives an empirical argument based on consensus (ijma') and tawatur (mass transmission): we know that Jews, Christians, and Muslims have unanimously reported the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. Then he argues that falsehood from such large groups is impossible because: (a) they could not have colluded through correspondence or conspiracy to lie—that would be impossible given their numbers and circumstances; (b) they could not have all gathered in one place to agree on the lie; (c) even if they gathered, collusion on a lie would go against the normal course of habit (adah)—it is not in human nature for such a large, diverse group to agree on a falsehood and keep it secret. Baqillani emphasizes that custom (adah) does not allow for a hundred thousand people to lie about a single report, though it might happen among one or two. Since these ways of falsehood are impossible for the vast multitude of transmitters of prophetic signs, the possibility of falsehood is negated, and their truthfulness is thereby proven. Finally, he adds a conditional: if it were possible for these transmitters to lie, then it would also be possible for the entire population of cities and countries to lie, which is absurd. Thus the argument stands as a proof for the veracity of the prophetic message.

تكليف
محنة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the primary reason that sending prophets is valid in God's wisdom?
A) It is necessary for God to send prophets.
B) It does not contradict God's attributes and brings great benefit.
C) It is impossible for God not to send prophets.
D) All religions agree on prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Sending prophets is rationally possible and wise because it brings immense reward without contradicting any of God's attributes or the system of trial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that sending prophets is a rational act of God's wisdom. But do you truly live as though every prophet, including Muhammad ﷺ, was sent for your own benefit—or do you sometimes treat their message as optional? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that prophethood is a mercy designed for your eternal reward?
?
Baqillani argues that prophethood does not corrupt obligation or annul trial. Have you ever used the excuse of human weakness or modern challenges to avoid obeying a prophetic command? How does knowing that prophethood perfectly preserves your trial and responsibility challenge that excuse?
?
If you truly believed that God's sending of messengers is a sign of His wisdom and care, would you approach the Quran and Sunnah with more eagerness? What specific step can you take today to show gratitude for this gift of prophethood?

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of Mass Collusion on Falsehood
Baqillani employs the tawatur argument to prove the truthfulness of the prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the unanimous transmission of prophetic miracles by Jews, Christians, and Muslims to prove the truthfulness of the prophets, based on the impossibility of such large groups conspiring to lie.

Core Idea
Massive consensus on a report makes falsehood impossible, thus proving the prophets true.

After establishing the rational possibility of prophethood, Baqillani provides a second, empirical proof: the fact that Jews, Christians, and Muslims have all transmitted the miracles of their prophets. He argues that it is impossible for such large, diverse groups to agree on a lie, whether through collusion or independent motivation. This impossibility proves the truth of their reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and note the three communities
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Baqillani mentions Jews, Christians, and Muslims as unanimous transmitters of the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.
2
Identify the three hypothetical ways falsehood could occur
Baqillani considers three ways falsehood could theoretically arise: via correspondence and writing, via gathering in one place, or via independent agreement without collusion.
3
Understand why each is impossible
He argues that each of these is impossible given the large numbers and diversity of the transmitters, relying on the normal course of custom (adah). Custom dictates that such groups cannot coordinate or independently agree on a falsehood.
4
Grasp the conclusion and the conditional argument
Since falsehood is impossible through any possible means, the reports must be true. He adds a conditional: if falsehood were possible here, it would be possible for all transmitters of cities, which is absurd.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tawatur argument only proves the Qur'an's authenticity.
Correction: Baqillani uses it for the foundational miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad, transmitted by Jews, Christians, and Muslims.

A) Because God directly prevents it.

Key Takeaway
The unanimous transmission of prophetic miracles by three large religious communities, combined with the impossibility of mass collusion on falsehood, proves the truth of the prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just encountered a logical proof that the prophets were truthful based on the unanimous testimony of millions across centuries. Do you actually feel that certainty in your heart, or have you been relying on blind imitation? What would it take for you to own this proof personally?
?

?

Module 6 · Lesson 38

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
Proof of Prophethood from the Impossibility of Mass Falsehood
Imam Baqillani argues that unanimous transmission precludes conspiracy to lie

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani uses the impossibility of mass falsehood to prove the truthfulness of prophetic reports and establish the prophethood of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Mass consensus in transmission makes falsehood impossible by custom.

This lesson presents one of the most powerful arguments in Islamic theology for the truthfulness of prophetic miracles. Imam Baqillani argues that when Jews, Christians, and Muslims all agree on the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad, it is impossible that they could have conspired to lie. The argument relies on the nature of human custom ('adah): large groups cannot collude to spread a falsehood consistently. This establishes the reliability of tawatur (mass transmission) and thereby proves prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Focus on the flow of the argument from consensus to impossibility to conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp the logical steps.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Baqillani grounds his argument in 'custom' ('adah). Custom does not allow a large group to conspire to lie. Understand that this is not a logical impossibility but a customary one.
This distinguishes his proof from purely rational arguments.
3
Examine the refuted scenarios
Baqillani considers two ways a large group could potentially lie: through correspondence or by gathering in one place. He argues both are impossible under custom. Try to see why each is considered impossible.
The text uses terms like 'tarasul' (correspondence) and 'ijtima'' (gathering).
4
Understand the absurd consequence
If mass falsehood were possible, then we could not trust any historical report, even the existence of well-known places like Khurasan. Baqillani uses this absurdity to reinforce the impossibility. Recognize this as a reductio ad absurdum.
This shows the practical necessity of trusting consensus transmissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على جواز إرسال الله الرسل وأنه قد فعل ذلك عِلْمُنا بأن اليهود والنصارى والمسلمين قد أطبقوا على نقل أعلام موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلّى الله عليه وعليهم وأن الكذب مستحيل جوازه على مثلهم من ناحية التراسل والتكاتب والمواطأة على ذلك لأن تمام ذلك وانتظامه من مثلهم محال مُتَعَذِّرٌ في العادة ومحال أيضاً جوازه على مثلهم بأن يجتمعوا جميعاً في بُقْعَةٍ واحدة من حيث يُشاهِدُ بعضُهم بعضاً ويتوافقوا على الكذب ونقله وإذاعته لأن اجتماع مثلهم في بقعة واحدة متعذر في مُسْتَقَرِّ العادة ولو أمكن أيضاً اجتماعهم لتَعَدّرَ في مُستقَرِّ العادة تَوَاطُوهم على الكذب ونقله واسْتِتارُ ذلك منهم وانْكِتامُه عليهم لأن العادة موضوعة على خلاف ذلك ويستحيل أيضاً وقوع الكذب من جماعةِ مَنْ ذكرنا من أعلام الرسل باتفاق وقوعه ؛ لأن العادة لم تجر باتفاق وقوع الكذب في مائة ألف إنسان عن مُخْبَرٍ واحد لداع واحد ودواع متفرقة وإن جاز ذلك الواحد والاثنين والنفر اليسير وليس يمكن وقوع الكذب من هذه الجماعات إلا على هذه الوجوه فإذا امتنعت فَسَدَ جواز الكذب عليهم وفي فساد ذلك إيجاب صدقهم فيما نقلوه وصحة ما إليه ذهبنا ولو أمكن وقوع الكذب مِنْ جميع من ذكرناه نَقَلَةِ أعلام الرسل على بعض هذه الوجوه أو غيرهـا لداع واحد أو دواع متباينة لأمكن وقوعه من نَقَلَةِ الأمصار والبلدان والممالك من السِّيرِ ولم نأمن ألا تكون في العالم بلدة تدعى خراسان والنَّهْرَوان والبَرَدان ولجاز جحد ما نأى وقَرُب منا البلدان وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على صحة إثبات نبوة الرسل عليهم السلام
And among what indicates the permissibility of God sending messengers and that He has done so is our knowledge that the Jews, Christians, and Muslims have unanimously agreed upon the transmission of the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace and blessings be upon him and them), and that falsehood is impossible in its occurrence among the likes of them in terms of correspondence, correspondence, and collusion upon that, because the completeness and order of that from the likes of them is impossible and hindered by custom, and also impossible in its occurrence among the likes of them that they all gather in one spot where some see others and agree upon falsehood, its transmission, and its dissemination, because the gathering of the likes of them in one spot is hindered in the settled custom, and even if their gathering were possible, their collusion upon falsehood and its transmission and the concealment of that from them and its hiding from them would be hindered in the settled custom, because custom is established contrary to that. And also the occurrence of falsehood from the group of those we mentioned among the miracle-bearing messengers, by agreement of its occurrence, is impossible; because custom has not run that the occurrence of falsehood from a hundred thousand people upon one informer with one motive or diverse motives is agreed upon, although that is possible for one or two or a small number. And the occurrence of falsehood from these groups is not possible except in these ways; so if they are prevented, the possibility of falsehood upon them is invalidated. And in the invalidation of that is the affirmation of their truthfulness in what they transmitted and the soundness of what we have held. And if the occurrence of falsehood from all whom we mentioned among the transmitters of the miracle-bearing messengers were possible in some of these ways or others, whether from one motive or diverse motives, then its occurrence from the transmitters of the cities, countries, and kingdoms through the biographies would be possible, and we would not be secure that there is not in the world a city called Khurasan, al-Nahrawan, and al-Baradan, and it would be permissible to deny what is far and near from us of the cities. And in the nullity of that is a proof of the soundness of establishing the prophethood of the messengers, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the truthfulness of prophets based on the impossibility of mass falsehood in the transmission of their miracles. The author begins by stating that the Jews, Christians, and Muslims all agree on the transmission of the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. He argues that it is impossible for such large and diverse groups to conspire to lie, because custom prevents a large group from colluding to propagate a falsehood. He considers several scenarios: that they might gather in one place and agree, or that they might correspond and collude. All these are impossible under normal custom. Even if a few individuals might lie, a large group like a hundred thousand cannot all agree to lie about the same report. If such mass falsehood were possible, then we could never trust any reports from distant lands, and even the existence of well-known places like Khurasan could be denied. Since that is absurd, the truthfulness of the transmitters of prophetic miracles is established, and thus prophethood is proven.

Key Terms
أعلام الرسل
الكذب
العادة
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of mass falsehood is a purely logical proof that cannot be violated.
Correction: Imam Baqillani grounds the impossibility in custom ('adah), not absolute logic. He argues that human nature and social norms prevent large groups from successfully conspiring to spread a falsehood, but this is not a metaphysical necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Baqillani, why is it impossible for the transmitters of prophetic miracles to have all lied?
A) Because they were all prophets and infallible
B) Because custom prevents a large group from conspiring to lie
C) Because God directly prevented them from lying
D) Because they were all from different religions

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is proven by the impossibility of mass falsehood in the transmission of miracles, grounded in the nature of human custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle reported by millions, do you instinctively trust it, or do you harbor doubts about a possible conspiracy? How does Baqillani's argument challenge the very doubts that arise from your own experience of small-scale deception?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about something because 'everyone said so'. Now compare that to the certainty required to believe in prophethood. Are you willing to base your entire worldview on the same kind of consensus that guarantees the existence of distant cities? If not, what is stopping you?
?
Baqillani says that if mass falsehood were possible, we couldn't even trust the existence of Khurasan. Do you live as if you truly believe that the prophets' reports are as certain as the existence of a city? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that these reports cannot be false?

Brahmins' Partial Acceptance of Prophethood
Imam Baqillani notes that Brahmins who accept some prophets concede the possibility of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani uses the Brahmins' acceptance of some prophets to refute the idea that they completely deny the possibility of prophethood.

Core Idea
Even partial acceptance of prophethood proves the principle is valid.

Imam Baqillani addresses the position of some Brahmins who accept certain prophets like Adam or Abraham but reject those after them. He points out that by accepting some prophets, they have implicitly conceded that prophethood is possible and has occurred. This subtle argument turns their partial agreement into evidence for the possibility of sending messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a conditional structure: 'As for those who...'
2
Identify the two groups mentioned
The passage refers to Brahmins who accept Adam but reject later messengers, and those who accept Abraham but reject later messengers. Mark these two groups.
3
Understand the logical implication
The author argues that by accepting any prophet, these groups admit that sending messengers is permissible and has occurred. Their disagreement is only about specific individuals, not the principle of prophethood itself.
This is the key argument: partial acceptance implies the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المثبتون من البراهمة لنبوة آدم الجاحدون لمن بعده من الرسل والمثبتون لنبوة إبراهيم الجاحدون لمن بعده من الرسل فقد أقروا بجواز إرسال الرسل وأنه قد وُجِدَ ونُقل وإن خالفوا في نبوة قوم بأعيانهم وليس ذلك من قول مُحِيل الرسالة جُمْلَةً في شيء فيقال
As for those among the Brahmins who affirm the prophethood of Adam and deny those after him of the messengers, and those who affirm the prophethood of Abraham and deny those after him of the messengers, they have acknowledged the permissibility of sending messengers and that it has occurred and been transmitted, even if they disagree about the prophethood of specific individuals, and that is not from the statement of one who renders the message impossible entirely, so it is said...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage addresses the position of the Brahmins (a group of Indian origin). The author notes that some Brahmins accept the prophethood of Adam or Abraham but reject the messengers after them. He argues that by accepting some prophets, they have admitted the principle that God can send messengers and that it has actually occurred. Their disagreement is only about specific individuals, so they do not fall into the category of those who completely deny the possibility of prophethood. The text ends with 'so it is said', indicating the author is about to offer a response or argument against their partial rejection.

The Consistency Argument for Prophethood
Baqillani presents a reductio ad absurdum using geographical examples to prove prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a geographical consistency argument to prove that the reports of prophets from all lands are valid through tawatur.

Core Idea
Denying distant prophetic reports forces denial of nearby ones—an absurdity that proves prophethood.

Baqillani begins by setting up a reductio ad absurdum. He argues that if one could reject reports of prophets from far-off lands due to lack of direct observation, then one would also have to reject reports from nearby towns. Since the latter is clearly false, the original rejection is impossible. This establishes the principle of tawatur in prophetic transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the reductio
See how Baqillani proposes that denying distant reports leads to denying nearby ones, which is absurd.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf).
3
Understand the consequence
The falsity of the absurd conclusion proves that the original denial is invalid, thus establishing the validity of all such reports.
4
Link to prophethood
Since prophetic reports from all lands are accepted through tawatur, prophethood is established.
This argument is foundational for the epistemology of prophetic knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسِّيرِ ولم نأمن ألا تكون في العالم بلدة تدعى خراسان (۱) والنَّهْرَوان (۲) والبَرَدان (۳) ولجاز جحد ما نأى (٤) وقَرُب منا البلدان وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على صحة إثبات نبوة الرسل عليهم السلام (٥) . وسنقول في الكلام في الأخبار وأقسامها ونَصِفُ (٦) التواتر منها والآحاد وما يُعْلَم صِحْةُ مُخْبَرِه باضطرار وما يعلم بنظر واستدلال وأحوال المُخبرين (۷) عنه عند (۸) انتهائنا إلى الكلام في الإمامة والرد على اليهود قولاً بيناً إن شاء الله تعالى ومتى (١٠) ثَبَتَ صحة نَقَلةِ أعلام الرسل من المسلمين وغيرهم من أهل الملل علم بذلك ثبوت نبوتهم لأن الله سبحانه لا يظهر المعجزات ويخرقُ العـادات على الذي يدعى النبوة مع العلم بدعواهم عليه إلا للدلالة على صدقهم والشهادة بثبوت نبوتهم .
And if we did not fear that there might be in the world a town called Khurasan (1) and Nahrawan (2) and Baradan (3), then it would be permissible to deny what is far (4) and near of the towns, and in the falsity of that is evidence for the correctness of establishing the prophethood of the messengers, peace be upon them (5). And we will speak concerning the discussion of reports and their divisions, and we will describe (6) the mutawatir among them and the ahad, and what is known of the correctness of its content necessarily, and what is known by reflection and inference, and the conditions of the reporters (7) about him when (8) we reach the discussion of the imamate and the refutation of the Jews, a clear statement, God willing, the Most High (10). And when the correctness of the transmitters of the signs of the messengers from the Muslims and others from the people of religions is established, then by that the establishment of their prophethood is known, because God the Exalted does not manifest miracles and break customs upon the one who claims prophethood while knowing their claims upon him except to indicate their truthfulness and to witness to the establishment of their prophethood.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: a proof by absurdity for the acceptance of prophetic reports, and a preview of upcoming discussions. The author argues that if one could deny the reports of prophets from far-off lands like Khurasan due to lack of direct observation, then one would also have to deny reports from nearby towns like Nahrawan and Baradan, which is clearly absurd. The impossibility of this absurdity proves that the reports of all prophets, regardless of distance, must be accepted—thus establishing the validity of prophethood through tawatur (widespread transmission). The author then announces that he will discuss in detail the categories of reports (akhbar), distinguishing between mutawatir (mass-transmitted) and ahad (single-reporter), and explaining which knowledge is derived necessarily (daruri) and which by reflection and inference (nazari), as well as the conditions of the transmitters. This discussion will occur when he reaches the topic of imamate and the refutation of the Jews. He reiterates the core principle: once the reliability of the transmitters of the prophets' miracles (a'lam) is confirmed, the prophethood itself is proven, because God only performs miracles at the hands of those who claim prophethood, precisely to attest to their truthfulness. The author's argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum (burlan al-khalf) against anyone who would selectively reject prophetic reports based on geographical distance.

Key Terms
التواتر
الأخبار
الآحاد
المعجزات
الاضطرار
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is only about geography, not about theology.
Correction: Baqillani uses geography as a model for how tawatur works for prophetic reports.

Checkpoint
What logical method does Baqillani use in this passage?
A) Direct proof
B) Reductio ad absurdum
C) Analogical reasoning
D) Induction

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of denying reports from nearby towns proves that reports from all lands, including prophethood, are valid through tawatur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on reports of places you have never seen. What reports about your own faith do you passively accept without investigation? How does Baqillani's argument challenge you to examine the consistency of your beliefs?
?
If you were to doubt the prophethood of Muhammad based on distance in time, what other accepted historical facts would you have to doubt? Are you willing to be consistent in your skepticism?
?
Baqillani uses everyday geographical knowledge to prove a theological truth. What everyday knowledge do you possess that should logically lead you to accept prophethood, but you have not applied? What is holding you back from making that connection?

Preview of Report Categories and the Principle of Miracles
Baqillani previews his upcoming discussion on report types and reiterates the miracle-based proof of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's preview of his upcoming discussion on the types of reports (mutawatir, ahad) and how miracles authenticate prophethood.

Core Idea
God's miracles at the hands of prophets are the ultimate guarantee of their truthfulness.

After establishing the consistency argument, Baqillani announces that he will fully discuss the categories of transmitted reports (akhbar). He distinguishes between mutawatir (mass-transmitted) and ahad (single-reporter) reports, and between knowledge gained necessarily (daruri) and by reflection (nazari). He also notes that the reliability of transmitters of prophetic miracles—from Muslims and others—confirms prophethood, because God only performs miracles to attest to truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the preview
Read the portion where Baqillani says he will discuss reports and their divisions.
2
Identify the distinctions
Note the terms: mutawatir vs ahad, 'ilm daruri vs 'ilm nazari, and conditions of transmitters.
These are foundational for Islamic epistemology.
3
Understand the principle of miracles
Baqillani states that God only supports true prophets with miracles, and the transmission of those miracles confirms prophethood.
This is a key theological principle.
4
Reflect on the methodology
See how Baqillani combines transmitted reports with rational inference to establish prophethood.
