Initial Argument on Transmission of Jewish Signs
Baqillani argues against the possibility of Jewish transmission being false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the universal denial of Jewish transmission by various groups proves its falsity, and why this refutes the objection that such denial would nullify all knowledge.

Core Idea
If a transmission were true, all rational observers would necessarily accept it; their denial proves it false.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses an objection: if the Jews' transmission of their prophet's signs were false, then all reports and knowledge would be nullified. He turns the objection on its head, arguing that the very fact that so many groups deny the Jewish transmission shows it cannot be genuine. This lesson unpacks his logical move and its implications for the necessity of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: Baqillani presents an objection first, then gives an answer.
The passage uses the word 'اضطراراً' meaning necessarily – a key term.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if Jewish transmission were false, it would nullify all reports and knowledge. Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the response
Baqillani argues that if the transmission were true, all groups (atheists, Brahmins, etc.) would necessarily know it. Since they deny it, it cannot be true. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
Notice the list of groups: they represent all rational non-Jewish perspectives.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument shows that universal denial is evidence against authenticity. Ask yourself: why does Baqillani consider denial by these groups as proof?
The answer lies in the concept of 'اضطرار' (necessary knowledge).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثالهم من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس ونَقَلة البلدان وفي ذلك التعطيل للأخبار والعِلم بشيء من جهتها أصلاً وتجويز أن تكون اليهود اليوم كاذبة في قولها إن النقل أخذته عن حجة كَهِي وذلك ما لا خلاص منه . ويقال لهم : لو كان ما تنقله اليهود اليوم وتدعيه صحيحاً ومأخوذاً مثلها سلف حجة لعلم الملحدون والبراهمة وأهل التثنية والمجوس وأصحاب الطبائع والفلاسفة والمنجمون صحة نقلهم اضطراراً فلما لم يكن ذلك كذلك وكان سائر من ذكرناهم يَجْحَدُ نقلهم بطل أن يكون صحيحاً .
Like them from among the Jews, Christians, Magians, and transmitters of the lands, and in that is nullification of reports and knowledge of anything from them at all, and the allowing that the Jews today could be lying in their claim that the transmission was taken from a proof like it — and that is inescapable. And it is said to them: If what the Jews transmit today and claim were true and taken from their predecessors as a proof, then the atheists, Brahmins, dualists, Magians, naturalists, philosophers, and astrologers would necessarily know the validity of their transmission. But since that is not the case, and all those we mentioned deny their transmission, it becomes false that it could be true.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. Baqillani begins by describing a scenario where opponents argue that if the Jews' transmission of their prophet's signs were false, it would nullify all reports and knowledge, and would allow the possibility that the Jews are lying. He then provides the answer: if the Jewish transmission were truly reliable and taken from authoritative predecessors, then all other groups (atheists, Brahmins, Magians, etc.) would necessarily acknowledge its validity. But since these groups deny it, the transmission cannot be true. The argument is that universal denial by opponents disproves the claim.

Key Terms
نقل (transmission)
اضطرار (necessity)
جحد (denial)
دليل (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is denying all religious transmission as false.
Correction: Baqillani is only refuting a specific objection about Jewish transmission; he does not deny all transmission, only argues that universal denial disproves this particular claim.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the fact that Brahmins and Magians deny the Jewish transmission prove it is false?
A) Because they are all liars.
B) Because if it were true, they would necessarily know it.
C) Because the Jews themselves admit it is false.
D) Because the transmission is not from God.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument shows that a transmission's authenticity is disproved by its universal rejection among rational observers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani says that if the Jewish transmission were true, all rational observers would necessarily know it. Think of a truth you hold in your life—your own faith or a deeply held conviction. Are all rational observers compelled to agree with it? If not, does that mean it might be false? How do you respond to the discomfort this question creates?
?
The passage lists atheists, Brahmins, dualists, and others as denying the Jewish transmission. Have you ever considered that your own beliefs might be denied by equally rational people? Does Baqillani's argument force you to examine why you believe what you believe, or do you simply dismiss dissent? What would it take for you to doubt your own transmission of faith?
?
Baqillani's argument concludes that the transmission is false because it is not universally accepted. If you applied this test to your own faith, would it pass or fail? And if it would fail, does that unsettle you? Are you willing to ask yourself what proof you have that your faith is not like the Jewish transmission described here?

The Dialectical Refutation of Denial Despite Necessary Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the objection that opponents know necessarily but deny.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical strategy for refuting the objection that opponents might know the truth necessarily but deny it stubbornly.

Core Idea
A denial of knowledge does not refute its necessity; the same logic applies to all claims.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a subtle objection: what if the opponents know the transmission necessarily but simply deny it out of stubbornness? He responds by applying the same logic to the objectors themselves, showing that their argument proves too much and undermines their own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the objection and response.
Notice the repeated structure: 'If they say... it is said to them...'
2
Identify the two exchanges
The passage contains two objection-response pairs. Find where the first objection ends and the second begins. Pay attention to the shift in the opponent's claim.
The first exchange deals with the claim of knowing but denying; the second deals with the claim of not finding knowledge within themselves.
3
Analyze the analogy
Baqillani uses an analogy: he applies the same logic to the objectors' own case. Ask yourself why this is effective. What does it show about the opponent's position?
The analogy turns the objection into a self-defeating argument.
4
Reflect on the dialectical power
Consider why Baqillani's response is a refutation rather than just a tu quoque. How does it undermine the opponent's criterion for knowledge?
This step ties to the learning objective: understanding dialectical strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : هم يعلمون ذلك ضرورةً ولكنهم يجحدون ما يعلمون قيل لهم : وكذلك أنتم وسائر النصارى عالمون بصحة نقل المسلمين لأعلام نبيهم غير أنكم تجحدون ذلك على علم منكم لِصِحَتِه فإن قالوا: نجد أنفسنا بخلاف ما تدعون ، قيل لهم : وكذلك تزعم البراهمة والمجوس والفلاسفة وأهل الإلحاد أنهم يجدون أنفسهم غير عالمين بصحة نقلكم؛ فلم يجب تصديقكم؛ ولا جواب لهم ذلك.
If they say: They know that necessarily, but they deny what they know. It is said to them: Likewise, you and all Christians know the validity of the Muslims' transmission of their prophet's signs, yet you deny that knowingly due to its validity. If they say: We find ourselves contrary to what you claim. It is said to them: Likewise, the Brahmins, Magians, philosophers, and atheists claim that they find themselves not knowing the validity of your transmission; so your credibility cannot be accepted, and they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage continues the objection-and-answer format. The opponent objects that the Brahmins and others may know the Jewish transmission necessarily but still deny it out of stubbornness. Baqillani replies that the same can be said about the Jews and Christians: they know the truth of the Muslims' transmission but deny it knowingly. If the opponent then says they do not find themselves knowing it, Baqillani retorts that the Brahmins etc. make the same claim about the Jewish transmission, so the opponent's argument is self-defeating.

Key Terms
ضرورة (necessarily)
جحد (denial)
علم (knowledge)
صحة (validity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage claims that all deniers are knowingly lying.
Correction: It is not a claim about psychology but a dialectical rebuttal showing the opponent's argument is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond to the claim that opponents know the truth but deny it?
A) He admits it is possible.
B) He says the same applies to Jews and Christians regarding Muslim transmission.
C) He ignores the objection.
D) He says only Muslims know the truth.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's refutation works by turning the opponent's own argument against them, showing that it self-destructs when applied consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Baqillani shows that an opponent's own criterion can be self-destructive. Is there any area of your life where you demand proof from others but exempt yourself from that same standard? How will you correct that starting today?

Inference from Silence: Defending Prophetic Signs
Baqillani addresses the objection that knowledge comes from inference, not necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the opponent's own inferential reasoning to defend knowledge of both Moses' and Muhammad's signs.

Core Idea
Inference from silence applies equally to all prophets' signs.

The opponent argues that they know Moses' signs not by necessity but by inference from the silence of contemporaries. Baqillani turns this argument back on them: if that inference is valid, it works equally for Muhammad's signs. This passage shows the consistency of his method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear through the words of Baqillani.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says they know Moses' signs through inference (istidlāl) based on the silence of those who could have denied the transmission, not through necessary knowledge.
Notice they concede they lack necessary knowledge.
3
See Baqillani's counter-application
Baqillani states that exactly the same reasoning applies to the signs of Muhammad (peace be upon him). The opponent's failure is due to neglecting proper investigation (nazar).
This is a classic refutation: if your method is sound, it must work consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن هم سكت قالوا : ليس نعلم صدق السلف الذين نقلوا أعلام موسى اضطراراً وإنما نعلم ذلك من أمرهم استدلالاً لسكوت من عن إنكار ما نقلوه مع ادعاء حضورهم ومشاهدتهم ومن صل عن النظر في ذلك جَهِلَ الحقِّ فيما نقلوه قيل لهم مِثْلُ ذلك في العلم بصحة كثير من أعلام النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأنها معلومة بمثل هذا الاستدلال وأنهم إنما جَهلوا لتركهم النظر فيما يدل على صحته .
And if they remain silent, they say: We do not know the truth of the forefathers who transmitted Moses' signs necessarily; rather we know that from their affair by inference, because of the silence of those who could deny what they transmitted, given their claim of presence and observation. And whoever refrains from considering that has missed the truth in what they transmitted. It is said to them: The same applies to knowledge of the validity of many of the signs of the Prophet (peace be upon him) — they are known by such inference, and they only missed it by neglecting consideration of what indicates its validity.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
Here the opponent reluctantly admits they do not have necessary knowledge but claim inferential knowledge based on the silence of contemporaries who could have denied the transmission. Baqillani responds that exactly the same inferential method applies to Muhammad's signs: Muslims know them through the same kind of inference from silence. The opponent's failure to accept them is due to their own neglect of proper investigation.

Key Terms
استدلال (inference)
سكوت (silence)
مشاهدة (observation)
نظر (consideration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage rejects inference as a valid source of knowledge about prophets.
Correction: Baqillani does not reject inference; he uses it consistently to defend knowledge of both Moses' and Muhammad's signs.

Checkpoint

A) Necessity.
B) Inferential reasoning from silence.
C) Direct revelation.
D) Consensus of all religions.

Key Takeaway
Valid inference from silence applies equally to all prophetic traditions; rejecting it for one prophet undermines it for all.

Consistency in Consensus Arguments
Baqillani refutes an objection on consensus with a consistency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the principle of logical consistency to refute an objection that relies on consensus of different religions.

Core Idea
Consensus of different religions proves nothing if it equally supports rejection.

Baqillani now faces an objection that the agreement of Jews, Christians, and Muslims on Moses' signs authenticates his prophethood, but no such agreement exists for Islam. His response is a masterful reductio: if consensus among differing groups can prove truth, it should also prove falsehood when they agree on rejection. This exposes the flaw in the opponent's reasoning and reinforces the need for consistent criteria.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the objection carefully: the opponent claims that the consensus of Jews, Christians, and Muslims on the validity of Moses' signs is proof, but this consensus does not exist for the Muslim report. Identify the claim.
The opponent assumes that consensus of different religions is a valid criterion.
2
Baqillani's Counter-Argument
Baqillani points out that there is also a consensus among Brahmins, Magians, philosophers, and materialists in rejecting those same signs. If consensus among differing groups is valid, then both acceptance and rejection should be equally proven.
3
Grasp the Logical Inconsistency
The opponent cannot have it both ways: if consensus of different religions on acceptance proves truth, then consensus on rejection must prove falsehood. This inconsistency shows the criterion is flawed.
Baqillani does not reject consensus outright; he exposes its misuse.
4
Reflect on the Lesson
Consider why consistency is important in theological arguments. This passage teaches that a principle applied to one side must be applied equally to the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب صحة نقل أعلام موسى والانقياد له لإطباق أهل الأديان المختلفة عليه كاليهود والنصارى والمسلمين وهذه العلة مفقودة مِنْ خَبَرِكم ، قيل لهم : لِمَ وَجَبَ ذلك دون أن يجب بطلانــه وتكذيبـه لاجتماع أهل الأديان المختلفة على تكذيبه ورده كالبراهمة والمجوس والفلاسفة وضروب الدهرية؟ فإن كان إطباق المختلفين في دياناتهم على تكذيب المُخبِرِ لا يدل على كذبه فما أنكرتم أن يكون إطباق المختلفين في دياناتهم على تصديق المخبرين لا يدل على صدقهم؟ .
If they say: The validity of the transmission of Moses' signs and submission to it is only necessary because of the consensus of people of different religions upon it, like Jews, Christians, and Muslims, and this cause is absent in your report. It is said to them: Why is that deemed necessary, without it being necessary that it is false and rejected because of the assembly of people of different religions upon its rejection and denial, like Brahmins, Magians, philosophers, and types of materialists? For if the consensus of those differing in religions upon the rejection of a reporter does not indicate his falsehood, then why do you deny that the consensus of those differing in religions upon affirming reporters does not indicate their truth?
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: that the agreement of multiple religious groups (Jews, Christians, Muslims) on Moses' signs proves their validity, but no such agreement exists for Islam. Baqillani counters that there is also agreement among other groups (Brahmins, Magians, etc.) in rejecting those same signs. If consensus among differing groups can prove truth, it should also prove falsehood when they agree on rejection. The argument exposes inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms
إطباق (consensus)
إجماع (agreement)
تكذيب (rejection)
تصديق (affirmation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani rejects consensus as a proof for prophethood.
Correction: Baqillani does not reject consensus; he shows that the opponent's application is inconsistent. He accepts that consensus can be used but argues it must be applied equally.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani challenge the claim that consensus of different religions proves Moses' signs?
A) He denies there is any consensus.
B) He points out that other groups also agree in rejection.
C) He says consensus is irrelevant.
D) He says only Muslims have consensus.

Key Takeaway
A criterion like consensus must be applied consistently; selective application undermines the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you used a standard of evidence for someone else's argument that you would not accept for your own? Will you commit to identifying and removing such double standards starting today?
?
Do you ever reject a valid argument simply because it comes from a source you distrust, while accepting the same argument from a trusted source? How can you ensure you judge arguments on their merit, not their origin?
?
If you are consistent in applying your criteria, would your own beliefs survive the test? Pause to examine one belief you hold and ask: would I accept the same level of evidence if it were used against this belief?

Two Dialectical Responses on Transmission and Signs
Baqillani extends the argument to Muslim transmission and Jesus' signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the opponent's own reasoning to defend Muslim transmission and then to argue for the authenticity of Jesus' miracles.

Core Idea
Turn the opponent's logic against them to force a dilemma.

Baqillani delivers two final blows in this dialectical exchange. He first shows that Muslim transmission enjoys broad agreement, then traps his opponent with their own principle to validate Jesus' signs. The argument is tight and forces the opponent into an inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and English translation together. Notice the structure: two responses introduced by 'Then it is said to them'.
The first response addresses Muslim transmission; the second addresses Jesus' signs.
2
Identify the first argument
Baqillani points out that Muslims and the 'Isawiyya sect agree on Muslim reports. This should compel the opponent to accept them.
The opponent objects that the 'Isawiyya only follow Muslims, but Baqillani flips it.
3
Understand the counter-rebuttal
The opponent says the 'Isawiyya' transmission originates from Muslims, making them one group. Baqillani applies the same logic to Moses' signs from Jews and Muslims.
This shows the opponent's objection proves too much.
4
Examine the second argument
Baqillani then argues that by the opponent's own principle, Jesus' signs must be valid since Muslims, Christians, and 'Isawiyya all affirm them.
This creates a dilemma: accept Jesus' miracles (and become Christian) or reject the reasoning.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Notice how Baqillani uses internal inconsistencies rather than external proofs. This is a classic kalam strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم من وجوب ثبوت خَبَرِ المسلمين وصِدْقِهم لإطباقهم وإطباق العيسوية منكم على تصديقهم وهم أهل دينين مُخْتَلِفين ومِلَّتين مُتباينتين؟ فإن قالوا : العيوسية إنما أخذوا نقل أعلام محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم عنكم وأنتم في الأصـل فـرقـة واحدة قيل لهم : وكذلك المسلمون والنصارى إنما أخذوا نقل أعلام موسى عليه السلام عن أسلافكم وعنكم وأنتم في الأصل فرقة واحدة فلا تجب الحجة بنقلكم ثم يقال لهم : فيجب على موضوع اعتلالكم تصحيح آيات المسيح عليه السلام لإطباقنا والنصارى والعيسوية على صحتها فإن أجابوا إلى ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه تركوا اعتلالهم .
Then it is said to them: Why do you deny the necessity of establishing the report of Muslims and their veracity, due to the agreement of them and the 'Isawiyya among you upon their affirmation, while they are people of two different religions and two divergent creeds? If they say: The 'Isawiyya only took the transmission of the signs of Muhammad (peace be upon him) from you, and you are originally one sect. It is said to them: Likewise, the Muslims and Christians only took the transmission of the signs of Moses (peace be upon him) from your ancestors and from you, and you are originally one sect, so the proof is not established by your transmission. Then it is said to them: So upon the basis of your reasoning, it becomes necessary to validate the signs of the Messiah (peace be upon him) because of the agreement of us, the Christians, and the 'Isawiyya upon their validity. If they accept that, they abandon their religion; if they refuse, they abandon their reasoning.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This lesson contains two final responses. First, Baqillani points out that Muslims and the 'Isawiyya (a Christian sect) agree on Muslim transmission, so by the opponent's logic it should be accepted. The opponent replies that the 'Isawiyya derive their transmission from Muslims, so they are originally one group. Baqillani then applies the same logic: Muslims and Christians derive Moses' signs from Jewish ancestors, so they are also one group. The second response uses the opponent's own principle to argue that the signs of Jesus (miracles) must be validated, since Muslims, Christians, and 'Isawiyya all affirm them. If the opponent accepts, they become Christian; if they deny, they abandon their own reasoning. This is a dialectical closing trap.

Key Terms
إطباق (agreement)
عيسوية (Isawiyya)
آيات المسيح (signs of the Messiah)
اعتلال (reasoning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a concession to Christianity.
Correction: It is a dialectical trap. Baqillani does not accept Christianity; he uses the opponent's own principle to force a logical dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the outcome of Baqillani's second argument about Jesus' signs?
A) The opponent accepts Christianity.
B) The opponent is left with a dilemma: accept Jesus' signs or abandon their reasoning.
C) The opponent proves Jesus' signs are false.
D) Baqillani concedes the argument.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani traps the opponent by using their own standard of agreement to validate both Muslim reports and Jesus' miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that exposes inconsistency in your own beliefs, do you tend to hold onto your position at the cost of sound reasoning? What would it take for you to abandon a comfortable belief for the truth?
?
Baqillani used the opponent's own standard to corner them. Are you willing to apply the same standards to yourself that you demand of others? Where might you be failing to do so today?
?
This passage presents a dilemma: accept the logical conclusion or reject your reasoning. Have you ever faced such a moment where your faith was tested by a logical argument? How did you respond, and what does that reveal about your attachment to truth?

The Proof of Moses' Prophethood Existed Before Muslims and Christians
Baqillani argues that the proof from Moses' miracles was established by Jewish transmission alone, validating Muslim transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the proof of Moses' prophethood was established by Jewish transmission alone, which logically validates Muslim transmission of Muhammad's miracles.

Core Idea
If Jewish transmission alone sufficed for Moses, Muslim transmission alone suffices for Muhammad.

Baqillani presents a clever dilemma to his opponents. By asking whether the proof of Moses' miracles existed before Christians and Muslims, he forces them to either undermine their own religion or accept the same standard for Islam. This passage shows the logical consistency of Islamic arguments for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure of the question and the two possible answers.
The Arabic is dense but the translation clarifies the logical moves.
2
Identify the Dilemma
Notice that Baqillani presents two possible answers to his question. Identify each horn of the dilemma and its consequences.
The opponent must choose either 'no' or 'yes'.
3
Trace the Logical Conclusion
Follow the reasoning: if the opponent says 'no', they contradict their own beliefs about the Torah. If they say 'yes', they must accept the same standard for Muslim transmission.
4
Reflect on the Argument's Strength
Consider why this argument is effective. It uses the opponent's own premises to force a consistent position.
This is a classic example of qiyas al-tamthil (analogical reasoning) in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : خبرونا عن نقلكم أعلام موسى عليه السلام هل كانت الحجة قائمة به قبل وجود النصارى والمسلمين وإطباقهم معكم على النقل مع خلاف البراهمة لكم وسائر من ذكرناه؟ فإن قالوا : لا تركوا دينهم وأوجبوا سقوط فرض شريعة موسى عن كل برهمي ومجوسي وملحد وفلسفي وأنه لا حجة عليها قبل نقل المسلمين والنصارى لأعلام موسى وليس ذلك من قولهم؛ وإن قالوا قد كانت الحجة لازمة بنقل اليهود وحدهم مع خلاف من خالفهم مع كثرة عددهم واختلاف دياناتهم قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من قيام الحجة بنقل المسلمين لأعلام نبيهم صلى الله عليه وسلم مع خلاف من خالفهم من أهل الملل؟ فلا يجدون بداً من ترك ما تعلقوا به .
Then it is said to them: Inform us about your transmission of the signs of Moses (peace be upon him): was the proof established through it before the existence of Christians and Muslims and their unanimity with you upon the transmission, despite the opposition of the Brahmins to you and all those we mentioned? If they say: No, then they abandon their religion and necessitate the fall of the obligation of the Law of Moses upon every Brahmin, Magian, atheist, and philosopher, and that there is no proof upon it before the transmission of Muslims and Christians of the signs of Moses; and that is not of their saying. And if they say: The proof was binding through the transmission of the Jews alone, despite the opposition of those who opposed them, despite their great number and diversity of religions, it is said to them: Why do you deny the establishment of the proof through the transmission of the Muslims of the signs of their Prophet (peace be upon him), despite the opposition of those who opposed them from the people of religions? So they find no escape from abandoning what they clung to.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful argument that undermines the opponent's entire position. Baqillani asks a decisive question: Was the proof (hujja) from the transmission of Moses' miracles established before Christians and Muslims existed and before they confirmed that transmission? The opponent faces a dilemma. If they answer 'No', that the proof was not established by Jewish transmission alone, then they would have to admit that the Torah was not binding on Brahmins, Magians, atheists, and philosophers before Muslims and Christians validated it. This contradicts their own belief that the Torah was always binding. If they answer 'Yes', that the proof was established by Jewish transmission alone despite opposition from many groups (like the Brahmins), then consistency demands that they accept the same for Muslims: the proof of Muhammad's prophethood is established by Muslim transmission alone, despite opposition from people of other religions. Since they cannot deny one case while affirming the other, they are forced to abandon their objection. The argument shows that the validity of transmission does not depend on universal agreement, but on the reliability of the transmitters.

Key Terms
الحجة
النقل
البراهمة
المجوس
شريعة موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: For a transmission to be authoritative, it must be accepted by all people.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if that were the case, the Torah would not have been binding before Christian/Muslim confirmation, which is absurd. Authoritativeness depends on reliability of transmitters, not universal acceptance.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of Baqillani's question about whether the proof of Moses was established before Christians and Muslims?
A) To prove that Muslims invented the miracles of Moses.
B) To show that the transmission of Moses' miracles was not valid until Christians and Muslims confirmed it.
C) To force the opponent into a dilemma that ultimately supports the validity of Muslim transmission of Muhammad's miracles.
D) To argue that the Brahmins are correct in rejecting Moses.

Key Takeaway
The proof of prophethood stands on reliable transmission alone, regardless of universal acceptance, as shown by the precedent of Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What practical step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of the proof of prophethood so that you are not swayed by the rejection of others?

Baqillani's Dilemma on Temporal Priority of Jewish Transmission
Baqillani presents a dilemma to Jewish opponents about the temporal priority of the proof of Moses' miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a dilemma to argue that the proof of Muhammad ﷺ is established by Muslim transmission in the same way the Jews claim for Moses.

Core Idea
A dilemma forces the opponent to accept Muslim transmission or abandon their own religion.

Baqillani challenges Jewish opponents by examining the timing of the proof of Moses' miracles. He forces them into a dilemma: either the proof was established before Christians and Muslims existed, or it was not. Both options lead to consequences the Jews cannot accept, thereby making the case for the authority of Muslim transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully to follow Baqillani's argument.
Notice the two conditional statements (if they say no, if they say yes).
2
Understand the dilemma
Recognize that the argument presents two possibilities, each leading to a problematic conclusion for the Jews.
The first possibility (proof not established before Christians/Muslims) undermines the universal obligation of Moses' law.
3
Analyze the second horn
If the proof was established by Jewish transmission alone, then the same logic applies to Muslim transmission for Muhammad.
This forces consistency: accept both or reject both.
4
Reflect on the implication
The opponent is left with no coherent response, thus the proof for Muhammad's prophethood is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : خبرونا عن نقلكم أعلام موسى عليه السلام هل كانت الحجة قائمة به قبل وجود النصارى والمسلمين وإطباقهم معكم على النقل مع خلاف البراهمة لكم وسائر من ذكرناه؟ فإن قالوا : لا تركوا دينهم وأوجبوا سقوط فرض شريعة موسى عن كل برهمي ومجوسي وملحد وفلسفي وأنه لا حجة عليها قبل نقل المسلمين والنصارى لأعلام موسى وليس ذلك من قولهم ؛ وإن قالوا قد كانت الحجة لازمة بنقل اليهود وحدهم مع خلاف من خالفهم مع كثرة عددهم واختلاف دياناتهم قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من قيام الحجة بنقل المسلمين لأعلام نبيهم صلى الله عليه وسلم مع خلاف من خالفهم من أهل الملل؟ فلا يجدون بداً من ترك ما تعلقوا به .
Then it is said to them: Inform us about your transmission of the signs of Moses, peace be upon him: Was the proof established by it before the existence of Christians and Muslims and their agreement with you upon the transmission, despite the opposition of the Brahmins to you and all whom we have mentioned? So if they say: No, they abandon their religion and necessitate the dropping of the obligation of the law of Moses from every Brahmin, Magian, atheist, and philosopher, and that there is no proof for it before the transmission of Muslims and Christians for the signs of Moses, and that is not their statement. And if they say: The proof was binding by the transmission of the Jews alone despite the opposition of those who opposed them, amid great numbers and diversity of religions, it is said to them: What prevents you from establishing proof by the transmission of Muslims for the signs of their Prophet, peace be upon him, despite the opposition of those who oppose them among the people of religions? Then they have no escape from abandoning what they cling to.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reductio ad absurdum. The author challenges the Jews: was the proof of Moses' signs established by your transmission before Christians and Muslims existed? If they say no, that implies that prior to those groups, the proof was not established, which would mean the law was not obligatory on non-Jews, which the Jews would not accept. If they say yes, that the Jewish transmission alone was sufficient, then why deny Muslim transmission for Muhammad? The author forces them to accept Muslim transmission.

Key Terms
أعلام (a'lam)
حجة (hujja)
نقل (naql)
براهمة (Barāhima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about which religion came first historically.
Correction: The argument is about the self-sufficiency of a transmission chain as proof, not about historical priority.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would happen if the Jew says the proof of Moses was not established before Christians and Muslims?
A) The law of Moses would become invalid.
B) They would have to admit that the Brahmins were correct.
C) They would abandon their religion.
D) The proof would be established by Muslim transmission.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani forces the opponent into a dilemma that leaves no escape: either accept that proof was established before others (which undermines their exclusivity) or accept that transmission alone suffices (which then applies to Muhammad).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a logical dilemma in your own beliefs, do you honestly follow the reasoning wherever it leads, or do you try to escape by clinging to what you want to believe? This lesson forces the opponent to accept the consequence. What is one area in your life where you have an inconsistent standard—expecting others to accept something while rejecting it for yourself? Will you resolve it today?
?
The Jews in the dilemma could not have it both ways. Consider your own evidence for the truth of Islam: do you accept the same standard of proof for Muhammad's prophethood that you require for others? If not, what does that say about your commitment to truth?
?
Baqillani's argument rests on the principle that if a transmission is sufficient for proof, it must be accepted universally. Is your faith built on a standard that you would accept from others? Or do you make exceptions? Write a specific commitment to apply the same standard of evidence to your own beliefs that you demand of non-Muslims.

Dichotomy on Source of Transmission
Baqillani forces a dilemma about transmission origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a dichotomy about the source of transmission to argue for the prophethood of Jesus and Muhammad.

Core Idea
A dichotomy about transmission routes forces the Jews to admit prophethood.

In this passage, Baqillani presents a clever argument that traps the Jewish interlocutor in a logical dilemma. The issue is whether the transmission of Moses' miracles came from the Jews themselves or from Jesus and Muhammad. Either way, the opponent loses: if from Jews, it's unreliable; if from Jesus and Muhammad, it acknowledges their prophethood. This concise move is a classic example of kalam dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a question leading to two possible answers.
2
Identify the two horns of the dichotomy
Find the two options: either the transmission comes from the Jews (one class) or from Jesus and Muhammad (who received from God). Understand the consequence of each option.
The first option leads to unreliability, the second to acknowledgment of prophethood.
3
Reflect on the argument's force
Why is this dichotomy effective? Because it leaves no third possibility and each option undermines the Jewish position. Discuss how this forces the opponent to choose between losing the argument or admitting prophethood.
This is a classic 'saw' (dilemma) in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : هل يخلو نقل المسلمين والنصارى لأعلام موسى عليه السلام من أن يكون مأخوذاً في الأصل عنكم أو عن عيسى ومحمد اللذين لم يأخذا عنكم وإنما أخذا عن الله تعالى؟ فإن كانوا إنما أخذوا ذلك عنكم وأنتم في الأصل طبقة واحدة والحجة بقول الطبقة الواحدة غير ثابتة وإن كانوا أخذوا ذلك عن عيسى ومحمد اللذين لم يأخذا عنكم فقد أخذوا عن الله سبحانه وهذا إقرار منكم بنبوتهما
Then it is said to them: Is the transmission of Muslims and Christians for the signs of Moses, peace be upon him, free from being originally taken from you or from Jesus and Muhammad, who did not take from you but rather took from God, exalted? So if they only took that from you, and you are originally one class, and proof by the statement of one class is not established. And if they took that from Jesus and Muhammad, who did not take from you, then they took from God, glorified, and this is an acknowledgment from you of their prophethood.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a dichotomy: either the transmission of Muslims/Christians about Moses comes from the Jews, in which case it's just one class and not reliable, or it comes from Jesus and Muhammad, who received from God, which would be an admission of their prophethood. This forces the Jews to choose between losing the argument or acknowledging the prophethood of Jesus and Muhammad.

Key Terms
طبقة (tabaqa)
إقرار (iqrar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is about historical accuracy of Moses' miracles.
Correction: The argument is about the epistemological reliability of the transmission chain, not about whether the miracles happened.

B) They received it from God.
C) The Jews must accept Christianity.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dichotomy forces the opponent to either invalidate the transmission of Moses' signs or admit the prophethood of Jesus and Muhammad.

Is there a belief you hold intellectually but have not fully acknowledged in your actions? What would change if you truly acknowledged it?

Refutation of the Dhimma/Jizya Criterion
Baqillani refutes the Jewish dhimma/jizya criterion for transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the Jewish claim that being in dhimma and paying jizya is a necessary condition for reliable transmission, using reductio ad absurdum arguments.

Core Idea
A condition for transmission must be universal; dhimma/jizya fails this test.

In this passage, Baqillani tackles a specific Jewish objection about the reliability of their transmission. The Jews argue that their transmission is valid because they are in a state of humiliation and pay jizya, while Muslims are not. Baqillani shows that this criterion leads to absurd consequences, such as invalidating historical reports and making Christian transmission valid for Jesus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
2
Identify the Jewish claim
The Jews claim that their transmission is valid because they are in dar dhilla and pay jizya. Find this in the passage.
This is the premise that Baqillani attacks.
3
Follow the first reductio
Baqillani points out that this criterion would invalidate transmissions of cities and histories (because they come from non-dhimmi sources). Understand why this is absurd.
4
Follow the second reductio
If the criterion holds, Christian transmissions about Jesus would be valid (since Christians are in dhimma). This contradicts Jewish exclusivity claims.
5
Note the remaining arguments
Baqillani continues: before Jews were in dhimma, their own prophethood would be invalid; and the signs of Jesus are transmitted by both parties. Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence.
The argument is incomplete due to a page break.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب صحة نقل اليهود لأنهم في دار ذلة الجزية وليس كذلك المسلمون لأنهم ليسوا في دار ذلة ولا ممن يؤدي الجزية فيقال لهم : فلا يجب على قولكم إثبات نقل البلدان والسير لأنه ليس بوارد عن أهل ذلة وممن يؤدي جزية؛ ويجب لهذه العلة صحة نقل النصارى لأعلام عيسى عليه السلام لأنهم في دار ذلة وممن يؤدي الجزية فلا يجدون من ذلك بداً أو يتركوا اعتلالهم ويقال لهم : فيجب سقوط فرض اعتقاد نبوة موسى عليه السلام وصحة ما جاء به قبل أن يحصلوا في دار ذلة وتؤخذ منهم الجزية فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه أبطلوا هذا الشرط في صحة الأخبار . ويقال لهم : فيجب صحة أعلام المسيح لإطباقهم والعيسوية ونحن معهم على نقلها لأن الفريقين يؤدون جزية وهي في دار ذلة وكذلك يجب
So if they say: The validity of the transmission of the Jews is only necessary because they are in the abode of humiliation of the jizya, and Muslims are not like that because they are not in an abode of humiliation nor among those who pay the jizya, it is said to them: Then according to your statement, the establishment of the transmission of cities and histories is not necessary because it is not reported from people of humiliation and from those who pay jizya; and for this reason, the validity of the transmission of the Christians for the signs of Jesus, peace be upon him, is necessary because they are in the abode of humiliation and among those who pay the jizya. So they find no escape from that or they abandon their pretext. And it is said to them: Then it is necessary that the obligation of believing in the prophethood of Moses, peace be upon him, and the validity of what he brought falls away before they came to be in the abode of humiliation and the jizya is taken from them. If they persist in that, they abandon their religion; and if they refuse, they invalidate this condition in the validity of reports. And it is said to them: Then the validity of the signs of the Messiah is necessary because of the agreement of them and the 'Isawiyya and we with them upon its transmission, for the two parties pay the jizya and are in the abode of humiliation; and likewise it is necessary...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage refutes the Jewish criterion for reliable transmission: being in a state of humiliation (dhimma) and paying jizya. Baqillani shows that this criterion would invalidate historical reports from non-dhimmi lands, would validate Christian transmission for Jesus, and would imply that before Jews were in dhimma, their own transmission was invalid. This is a reductio ad absurdum. The argument is cut off at the end of the page but clearly aims to further press the inconsistency.

Key Terms

جزية (jizya)
شرط (shart)

Misconception: The validity of transmission depends on the transmitters being in a state of humiliation (dhimma) and paying jizya.
Correction: This criterion leads to contradictions: it would invalidate historical reports, validate Christian transmissions, and undermine Jewish prophethood before dhimma. Thus, it cannot be a valid condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The 'Isawiyya Objection and Symmetry Argument
Baqillani presents an objection and responds with a symmetry argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the consensus of the 'Isawiyya to defend the transmission of Muhammad's signs and refutes an objection using a symmetry argument.

Core Idea
Baqillani turns the opponents' own criterion against them to defend prophetic transmission.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a potential objection against the reliability of the Muslims' transmission of the signs of Prophet Muhammad. He shows that the same standard used to challenge Muslims would also undermine the opponents' own claims about Moses. This dialectical move exemplifies the use of rational consistency in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without interpretation. Note the key terms like العيسوية, أعلام, نقل, and فرقة.
The passage is a single sentence, so read it carefully.
2
Understand the initial assertion
Baqillani begins by asserting the validity of Muslim transmission because the 'Isawiyya (a Christian sect) unanimously transmit it, despite their low status.
The 'Isawiyya are described as أهل ذلة (people of humiliation) and payers of jizya.
3
Identify the objection
The opponents object: 'You (Muslims) took this transmission from a single group (the 'Isawiyya), and a single group's transmission is not proof.'
The phrase فرقة واحدة is key.
4
Analyze the symmetry response
Baqillani responds that Muslims and Christians took the transmission of Moses' signs from the Jews, who are also a single group. If the opponents reject single-group transmission, their own claim about Moses becomes invalid.
Notice the parallel: just as Muslims rely on 'Isawiyya, Jews rely on their own group.
5
Draw the conclusion
The objection is self-defeating. Baqillani demonstrates consistency: the opponents' own criterion would undermine their beliefs.
This is a classic dialectical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صحة نقل المسلمين لأعلام محمد عليه السلام لإطباق العيسوية على نقلها وهم أهل ذلة وممن يؤدي الجزية فإن قالوا عنكم أخذوا هذا النقل وأنتم في الأصل فرقة واحدة قيل لهم : وكذلك المسلمون والنصارى ومحمد وعيسى عليهما السلام إنما أخذوا النقل لأعلام موسى عنكم وأنتم في الأصل طبقة واحدة ونقل الفرقة عندكم لا تقوم به الحجة فبطل نقلكم
The validity of the Muslims' transmission of the signs of Muhammad (peace be upon him) because of the consensus of the 'Isawiyya on its transmission, and they are a people of humiliation and among those who pay the jizya. If they say: 'You took this transmission from you while you are originally one group,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, the Muslims and the Christians, and Muhammad and Jesus (peace be upon them) only took the transmission of the signs of Moses from you while you are originally one class. The transmission of a (single) group according to you does not constitute proof, so your transmission is invalid.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the transmission of prophetic signs. Baqillani begins by asserting the validity of the Muslims' transmission of the signs (a'lam) of Prophet Muhammad. He supports this by noting the consensus of the 'Isawiyya (a Christian sect) on transmitting those signs, despite the 'Isawiyya being a humble people who pay the jizya. Then he anticipates an objection: the opponents might argue that the Muslims took this transmission from a single group (the 'Isawiyya), and a single group's transmission is not authoritative. The response points out symmetry: the Muslims and Christians, including Muhammad and Jesus, took the transmission of Moses' signs from the Jews, who are also a single group. Since the opponents consider the transmission of a single group insufficient, their own transmission of Moses' signs would be invalid. Thus the objection is refuted by turning it against the objectors.

Key Terms
العيسوية
أعلام
نقل
فرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani argues that single-group transmission is always invalid.
Correction: He only shows that if the opponents reject single-group transmission, they must also reject their own transmission of Moses' signs.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the validity of Muslim transmission of Muhammad's signs in this passage?
A) The consensus of Jews
B) The consensus of the 'Isawiyya
C) The consensus of all Christians
D) The consensus of the Muslims themselves

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Muslim transmission by showing that the opponents' objection would invalidate their own claims about Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani uses the opponents' own criteria against them. Is there a truth you reject because of its source, while accepting a similar claim from a source you prefer? Will you honestly examine that inconsistency today?
?
The 'Isawiyya were humiliated, yet their transmission is used as evidence. Does your faith rest on the strength of its evidence or on the social status of those who present it? If the truth came from a despised group, would you still accept it?
?
You have heard the signs of Muhammad through a tradition. Have you ever critically examined that transmission, or do you rely on it simply because it is familiar? This lesson challenges you to apply the same standard to your own beliefs that you apply to others. What will you do about that?

Refuting the Testimony Objection: The Symmetry Argument
Baqillani refutes the claim that testimony for Moses' signs does not establish Muhammad's signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a symmetrical argument to refute the Jewish claim that their testimony for Moses' signs is stronger than Muslim testimony for Muhammad's signs.

Core Idea
Multiple testimonies on a single source do not create independent proof.

In this passage, Baqillani continues his dialectical exchange with a Jewish opponent. The opponent raises a clever objection: Muslims and Christians have testified to the validity of Moses' signs, which strengthens the Jewish claim, but Muslims have not testified to Muhammad's signs, suggesting a weakness. Baqillani responds by dissecting the nature of testimony and turning the argument back on the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the opponent's claim: that the testimony of Muslims and Christians for Moses' signs is like evidence for the Jewish claim, while Muslims do not testify for Muhammad's signs.
Notice the opponent uses the word 'shahida' (testify) to frame their argument.
2
Analyze Baqillani's first response
Baqillani says the testimony of Muslims and Christians is 'testimony upon your testimony'—it depends on the Jewish original transmission. A multiplicity of testimonies on a single original testimony does not constitute a proof or evidence.
The key concept is that the chain of testimony goes back to one source (the Jews).
3
Examine the symmetrical response
Baqillani turns the argument: Muslims and the 'Isawiyya (a Christian group) testify to Jesus' signs, so by the opponent's logic, that should be established. The opponent counters that this is also a single testimony, and Baqillani replies that the same applies to their own adduced testimony.
This demonstrates the dialectical technique of arguing by parity of reasoning.
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
The passage shows how Baqillani neutralizes the objection by showing its internal inconsistency and applying the same standard to both sides.
This is a classic example of refuting an argument by turning it on itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : قد شَهِدْتُم وشهدت النصارى لنا بصحة أعلام موسى وذلك كالبينة على دعوانا ولم نَشْهَد لكم بصحة أعلام نبيكم، قيل لهم : وشهاداتنا وشهادة النصارى هي شهادة على شهادتكم وأنتم في الأصل فرقة واحدة وكثرة الشهادات على شهادة واحدة من واحد أو فرقة واحدة ليست بحُجَّةٍ ولا بَيِّنَةٍ ثم يقال لهم : وكذلك قد شهدنا نحن والعيسوية بصحة أعلام عليه السلام فيجب إثباتها عندكم فإن قالوا : شهادتكم على ذلك شهادة على شهادتهم وهي شهادة واحدة في الأصل قيل لهم : مِثْلُ ذلك فيما تَعَلَّقُوا به
If they say: 'You and the Christians have testified for us to the validity of the signs of Moses, and that is like evidence for our claim, and you have not testified for us to the validity of the signs of your prophet,' it is said to them: 'Our testimony and the testimony of the Christians is testimony upon your testimony, and you are originally one group. The multiplicity of testimonies upon a single testimony from one person or one group is not a proof nor an evidence.' Then it is said to them: 'Likewise, we and the 'Isawiyya have testified to the validity of his (Jesus') signs, so it must be established with you.' If they say: 'Your testimony on that is testimony upon their testimony, and it is a single testimony originally,' it is said to them: 'The same applies to what they have adduced.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectic. The opponents raise a new objection: they claim that the Muslims and Christians have testified to the validity of Moses' signs, which serves as evidence for the Jews' claim, but the Muslims have not testified to the validity of Muhammad's signs, implying that Muhammad's signs are less established. Baqillani's response has two parts. First, he argues that the testimony of the Muslims and Christians is actually testimony upon the Jews' own testimony (i.e., it depends on the Jews' original transmission). Since the Jews are a single group, a multiplicity of testimonies on a single original testimony does not constitute a proof or evidence. Second, he turns the argument: the Muslims together with the 'Isawiyya have testified to the validity of Jesus' signs, so by the same logic, that should be established with the opponents. When the opponents counter that this Muslims-'Isawiyya testimony is itself a single testimony (since it is based on the 'Isawiyya alone), Baqillani replies that the same holds for what the opponents themselves adduced (i.e., their own testimony is also a single testimony). Thus the objection is neutralized by showing the symmetry and the weakness of relying on testimony chains that depend on a single original source.

Key Terms
شهادة
بينة
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that multiple people testifying to the same event creates independent evidence regardless of source.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if the testimonies all depend on a single original source (like the Jews), they do not create independent proof; they are merely multiple attestations of the same original testimony.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say that the testimony of Muslims and Christians is not a proof?
A) Because they are not trustworthy
B) Because it is testimony upon a single original testimony from a single group
C) Because they do not agree with each other
D) Because the 'Isawiyya rejected it

Key Takeaway
Testimony chains that ultimately depend on a single original source do not constitute independent proof, a principle Baqillani uses symmetrically to refute the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you rely on the fact that many people believe something as a reason to accept it, without checking whether they all depend on the same original source? In what area of your faith might you be doing that right now? What is one step you can take to verify the foundation of that belief?
?
Baqillani uses symmetrical reasoning: what applies to one side applies to the other. Are you willing to apply the same standard of evidence to all religious claims, even those you are emotionally attached to? When have you been inconsistent? Ask Allah for fairness and repent.
?
This passage shows that a chain of testimony originating from a single source does not multiply into independent proofs. Your own connection to the Prophet (peace be upon him) comes through a chain of narrators. How does that dependence make you feel about the authenticity of your faith? What can you do today to strengthen your confidence in that preserved transmission?

The objection of compulsion by the sword and its refutation
Baqillani refutes the claim that Muslim transmitters were compelled by the sword.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that Muslim transmitters of Muhammad's miracles were compelled by the sword, thereby defending the reliability of Islamic transmission.

Core Idea
The sword did not compel transmission; the proof of miracles stands independently.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the transmission of Muhammad's miracles: that people were forced to accept them under threat. Baqillani dismantles this claim by challenging its premise and offering an alternative explanation. You'll see how he defends the integrity of Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection as presented in the first part of the passage. Notice how it compares the transmission of Moses' signs with Muhammad's signs.
The objector claims compulsion only for Muhammad's signs.
2
Identify Baqillani's first response
Find where Baqillani asks for proof of the claim and suggests it might be false. He notes that Muslims did not compel anyone to transmit the signs.
This challenges the burden of proof.
3
Understand the second response
Baqillani argues that even if one questioned the community, they would not find widespread knowledge of the signs, showing that the miracle (Quran) is self-evident, not dependent on transmission.
This shifts the ground from transmission to direct evidence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence clarifies that people are only asked to enter religion after the proof (the miracle) is established, not after verifying chains.
This reframes the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب القول بثبوت أعلام موسى عليه السلام لأن الناقلة لها لم يُحْمَلُوا على نقلهم بالسيف ونَقَلةُ أعلام محمد عليه السلام محمولون على النقل بالسيف، قيل لهم : ولم زعمتم أنا محمولون على النقل للأعلام بالسيف وما دليلكم على ذلك وما أنكرتم أن تكون هذه الدعوى كَذِباً لأننا لم نحمل أحداً أسلم وأقر بالشهادتين على نقل أعلام نبينا عليه السلام ؟ ولو اعترض مُعْتَرِضٌ جُمْهُورَ الأمة لم نَجِدْ عندها من نقل هذه الأعلام شيئاً ولا معرفة بكثير منها وإنما نطالبهم بالدخول في الدين بعد قيام الحجة فقط
If they say: 'The statement of the establishment of the signs of Moses (peace be upon him) is only necessary because the transmitters of them were not compelled to their transmission by the sword, while the transmitters of the signs of Muhammad (peace be upon him) were compelled to transmission by the sword,' it is said to them: 'Why do you claim that we are compelled to transmit the signs by the sword? And what is your proof for that? And why do you not consider that this claim might be false, because we did not compel anyone who embraced Islam and uttered the two testimonies to transmit the signs of our prophet (peace be upon him)? And if an objector were to confront the multitude of the community, we would not find with them any transmission of these signs nor knowledge of most of them. We only demand them to enter the religion after the establishment of the proof.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage addresses a major objection: that the transmitters of Moses' signs were not compelled by the sword, whereas the transmitters of Muhammad's signs were, thus the latter are not reliable. Baqillani's response is twofold. First, he challenges the premise: he asks for proof that Muslims were compelled, and suggests the claim might be false because Muslims do not force anyone to transmit the signs; rather, people embrace Islam freely. Second, he argues that if one were to interrogate the majority of the Muslim community, they would not find any transmission of these signs (i.e., the signs are not widely known by transmission) nor knowledge of most of them. This implies that the signs are not something people are forced to transmit; rather, the sign of Muhammad (the Quran) is a continuous miracle that does not rely on such transmission. The demand is only for people to enter the religion after the proof (the miracle) has been established, not after verifying chains of transmission. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
سيف
حمل
دعوى
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transmission of Muhammad's signs is unreliable because Muslims used the sword in conquest, implying that converts were forced to accept and transmit the miracles.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the transmission of signs (the Quran) was not coerced; rather, people embraced Islam freely. The miracle itself is the proof, not the chain of transmission.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani consider the claim of compulsion by the sword possibly false?
A) Because Muslims did not use the sword at all
B) Because Muslims did not compel anyone to transmit the signs
C) Because the 'Isawiyya also used the sword
D) Because the Jews did not use the sword

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the compulsion objection by challenging its premise and asserting that the miracle itself, not transmission, is the basis for entering Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief more because of social pressure or fear (the 'sword' of your environment) than because you were personally convinced by clear evidence? What would it take for you to examine your convictions solely based on proof, as Baqillani insists?
?
If the Quran is a self-evident miracle, why do you sometimes ignore its challenge and treat it as just a book of rules? What specific step will you take this week to confront its inimitability—perhaps reading a translation with new eyes or studying its linguistic miracle?

The Argument from Moses' Law and the Sword
Baqillani argues that Jews also faced compulsion under Moses' law of apostasy, so the objection is symmetric.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument of symmetric compulsion: if Jewish transmission is valid despite potential compulsion under Moses' law, then Muslim transmission is equally valid.

Core Idea
Jewish transmission is also under the sword, making the objection symmetric.

Baqillani now turns the opponents' objection on its head. He asks whether Moses' law also mandated death for apostasy. If so, the Jewish ancestors might have been compelled to transmit the signs out of fear, just as the opponents claim about Muslims. This sets up a symmetrical argument that neutralizes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question about Moses' law
Read how Baqillani asks whether Moses' law mandated killing apostates. Notice that this is a rhetorical question expecting a 'yes' from the opponents.
The opponents cannot deny this, as it is a fact in Jewish tradition.
2
Understand Baqillani's inference
If the opponents admit that Moses' law prescribed death for apostasy, then Jewish ancestors could have entered the religion for worldly motives and stayed only out of fear. Thus they were compelled to transmit the signs.
This turns the compulsion objection back on the opponents.
3
Examine the opponents' response
The opponents argue that compulsion only applied to staying in the religion, not entering it. So they claim their ancestors were not compelled to transmit.
This is a subtle distinction they hope will save their case.
4
See Baqillani's counter
Baqillani responds by describing Muslim practice: Muslims do not kill those who do not transmit the signs, whether they are Muslims, dhimmis, or treaty people. So Muslims are not compelled either. The symmetry is complete.
The argument does not rely on historical accuracy but on logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : أليس موسى عليه السلام كان من دينه وشريعته أن يقتل من ارتد عن دينه وفارق ملّته بعد الدخول فيها؟ فإذا قالوا : نعم ، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن تكونوا محمولين على نقل أعلام موسى عليه بالسيف وأن يكون أسلافكم الذين كانت منهم المُنة والرئاسة إنما دخلوا في دين موسى رَغْبَةً وحباً لأسباب الدنيا والتَّرَأْس فيها وضَمِنَ لهم ذلك فلما دخلوا في الدين لم يمكنهم الخروج منه خوفاً من القتل فصاروا محمولين على النقل ؟ فإن قالوا : لم يكن أسلافنا يحملون الناس على الدخول في الدين وإن حملوهم على المُقام عليه بعد الدخول فيه فلم يكونوا لذلك محمولين ؛ قيل لهم : وكذلك نحن لا نقتل من دخل في ديننا إذا لم ينقل أعلام نبينا ولا نقتل أيضاً من أدى الجزية وأقام على دينه ولم يدخل في ديننا إذا لم ينقل أعلام نبينا أو كان من أهل العَهْدِ والصلح فلم يجز أن يكونوا محمولين على نقل أعلام نبينا عليه السلام
Then it is said to them: 'Did not Moses (peace be upon him) have in his religion and law that he kills whoever apostatizes from his religion and leaves his community after entering it?' If they say: 'Yes,' it is said to them: 'So why do you deny that you were compelled to transmit the signs of Moses by the sword, and that your ancestors, who had authority and leadership, only entered the religion of Moses out of desire and love for worldly reasons and leadership, and that was guaranteed to them, and when they entered the religion they could not leave it out of fear of execution, so they became compelled to transmit?' If they say: 'Our ancestors did not compel people to enter the religion, though they compelled them to remain in it after entering, so they were not compelled,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, we do not kill whoever enters our religion if he does not transmit the signs of our prophet, nor do we kill whoever pays the jizya and remains in his religion and does not enter our religion if he does not transmit the signs of our prophet, or if he is from those who have a covenant and peace treaty. So it is not valid that they are compelled to transmit the signs of our prophet (peace be upon him).'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a new argument turning the tables on the opponents. Baqillani asks whether Moses' law mandated killing apostates. The opponents agree. Then he argues that the Jewish ancestors might have entered the religion for worldly motives (desire for power, leadership) and then were compelled to stay and transmit out of fear of execution. Thus they are also compelled by the sword. The opponents respond that their ancestors only compelled people to remain, not to enter, so they are not compelled. Baqillani counters by analogy: Muslims do not kill those who do not transmit the signs, whether they are Muslims who do not transmit, dhimmis (jizya payers), or people under treaty. Therefore, Muslims are not compelled either. The argument establishes symmetry: if Jewish transmission is valid despite potential compulsion, then Muslim transmission is equally valid.

Key Terms
ارتداد
سيف
جزية
عهد
صلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about historical accuracy of Jewish conversion motives.
Correction: The argument is about logical symmetry in the opponents' own framework, not about actual historical events.

Checkpoint
What is the point of Baqillani's question about Moses' law of killing apostates?
A) To prove that Moses was a prophet
B) To show that Jews also could be compelled by the sword
C) To criticize Moses' law
D) To argue that Muslims should kill apostates

Key Takeaway
The symmetry argument neutralizes the compulsion objection by showing it applies equally to Jewish transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief or practice only because you feared the social cost of leaving it, rather than out of genuine conviction? How does Baqillani's argument challenge you to examine the motives behind your own religious commitments?
?

?
Baqillani refutes an objection by showing its symmetrical flaw. Is there an area where you have been critical of others while ignoring the same flaw in yourself? What step will you take this week to align your criticism with self-reflection?

The 'Isawiyya Counterexample to Sword Compulsion
Baqillani argues that the 'Isawiyya's voluntary transmission validates Muhammad's signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the 'Isawiyya sect to refute the claim that Islamic transmission was compelled by the sword.

Core Idea
A voluntary transmitter undermines the sword compulsion argument.

Baqillani introduces a specific Christian sect, the 'Isawiyya, as a crucial counterexample. If they transmitted Jesus's signs without coercion, then the claim that all religious transmission is tainted by sword compulsion fails. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two parts: first, the transmission of Muhammad's signs via the 'Isawiyya; second, the transmission of Jesus's signs via the same group.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the argument is clear.
2
Identify the logical structure
Baqillani presents a conditional: If the 'Isawiyya transmitted without compulsion, then their transmission is valid. This validates both Jesus's signs and, by extension, Muhammad's signs.
3
Reflect on the rebuttal
Consider the opponent's possible objection and how Baqillani counters it: if they claim Christians were compelled, the same applies to Jews; and the 'Isawiyya specifically are not compelled.
This shows the power of a single counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم أيضاً: فيجب صِحَةُ نقل أعلام محمد بنقل العيسوية وهم أمة عظيمة لأنها لم تُحْمَلْ على ذلك بالسيف وكذلك يجب صحة نقل أعلام المسيح عليه السلام لنقلهم ونقل العيسوية لها وهم غير محمولين على النقل بالسيف فإن قالوا : النصارى محمولة على النقل بالسيف قيل لهم : وكذلك أنتم محمولون ، وقيل لهم : فالعيسوية غير محمولة على نقل أعلام
And it is also said to them: 'Thus the validity of the transmission of the signs of Muhammad is necessary through the transmission of the 'Isawiyya, and they are a great nation, because they were not compelled to that by the sword. Similarly, the validity of the transmission of the signs of Jesus (peace be upon him) must be through their transmission and the transmission of the 'Isawiyya for them, and they are not compelled to transmit by the sword.' If they say: 'The Christians are compelled to transmit by the sword,' it is said to them: 'Likewise, you are compelled.' And it is said to them: 'The 'Isawiyya are not compelled to transmit the signs...'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage offers a final argument to refute the compulsion claim. Baqillani points out that the 'Isawiyya (a Christian sect) transmitted the signs of Jesus, and they are a great nation that was not compelled by the sword. Therefore, their transmission is valid. By the same logic, the Muslim transmission of Muhammad's signs, which the 'Isawiyya also accept, must be valid because it is based on the same voluntary transmission. If the opponents claim that the Christians (i.e., the mainstream Christians) were compelled, then the response is that the Jews themselves are also compelled (as argued earlier). Furthermore, the 'Isawiyya specifically are not compelled. The passage is incomplete but sets up a strong counterexample: a group that transmitted without coercion validates the entire chain. This undermines the opponents' claim that transmission under compulsion is untrustworthy.

سيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that all Christians are not compelled by the sword.
Correction: The argument singles out the 'Isawiyya as a specific voluntary group, not all Christians.

D) Because they reject all transmission

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses the 'Isawiyya sect to show that not all transmission is compelled, thus refuting the sword compulsion objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to transmit that belief voluntarily, even if it cost you something?
?
Baqillani uses a specific group to show that not all transmission is compelled. When you share Islam with others, do you assume that their acceptance must be forced or that they are coerced? How can you instead present truth with the confidence that voluntary acceptance is possible, just as the 'Isawiyya accepted?
?
The 'Isawiyya are called a 'great nation' despite not being mainstream. Are you willing to stand with a small group or unpopular truth if it means upholding voluntary, sincere belief? What invisible 'swords' might you be submitting to instead of embracing the truth freely?
